Executive Summary
The Climate Resilient Irrigation for Sustainable Production (CRIPS) Project will support the preparation and
implementation of the National Irrigation Plan (NIP) in Ethiopia, transitioning the sector from the project-
based approach to a more comprehensive, program-based model. The Project is World Bank-financed and
will be implemented by the Ministry of Irrigation and Lowlands (MILLs) at federal level with regional
irrigation bureaus and collaborating agencies (Ministry of Water and Environment - MoWE for
dams/storage; Ministry of Agriculture - MoA for value chains).

The Project Development Objective (PDO) is to improve access to resilient irrigation services for
sustainable production in Ethiopia, which should be achieved through the following components;
Component 1: Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization; Component 2: Small-Scale Irrigation
Development; Component 3: Dam Safety and Irrigation Storage Improvement; and Component 4:
Irrigation Service Improvement.

This ESMF was prepared for Project Components , as specific project sites as well as detail designs are not
known yet. It presents measures and plans to reduce, mitigate and/or offset adverse risks and impacts,
provisions for estimating and budgeting the costs of these measures, and institutional arrangements and
capacities for the implementation of the E&S measures and presents a guiding framework for inclusive
and sustainable irrigation development in Ethiopia. The ESMF contains Labor Management Procedures
(LMP), a Sexual Exploitation and Abuse (SEA) and Sexual Harassment (SH) Action Plan, an Integrated Pest
Management Framework (IPMF), a Waste Management Plan (WMP), Chance Find Procedures, and a
Resettlement Framework (RF). A Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) has been prepared as a separate
document. E&S risks under Component 1, will also have an Environmental and Social Impact Assessments
(ESIA) as soon as the detail Project activities are identified (see TOR for Component 1 ESIAs in Annex 12).
A Strategic Environmental and Social Assessment (SESA) will be prepared for the Technical Assistance (TA)
activities of the Project as part of the National Irrigation Strategy, and a Security Risk Assessment and
Management Plan (SRAMP) will be prepared for the general implementation of the Project.

The key national environmental and social legislation includes the Environmental Impact Assessment
Proclamation No. 299/2002, and other Proclamations on Solid Waste Management, Water Resource laws,
Labor Laws, gender policies and regulations stipulating public participation and others. The ESMF is further
based on the World Bank Environmental and Social Framework (ESF), specifically on the Environmental
and Social Standard (ESS) 1 – ESS8 and ESS10. As the E&S risks of the Project are classified as substantial,
the Project will be based on the World Bank ESSs, as well as on national legislation. Where they differ, the
more stringent will apply.

The Project is anticipated to have significant positive impacts. The project targets improved irrigation for
farmers and with that aims to improve livelihoods, as well as enhances community safety through dam
safety initiatives.

The environmental and social risk classification for the project is Substantial. The environmental risks and
impacts of the Project are mainly associated with the rehabilitation or construction of large, medium and
Small Scale Irrigation (SSI) infrastructre, the rehabilitation of dams and the construction of small dams and
haffirs. The rehabilitation and modernization of irrigation schemes could lead to altered hydrological
regimes, affecting downstream water availability and potentially impacting aquatic ecosystems. Some
activities include construction-related risks and impacts, such as air pollution and dust emission during the



                                                                                                           6
                                                                                                         Official Use Only
works; soil and water contamination and degradation of water bodies through the discharge of waste;
pollution of local surface water sources; generation of domestic and construction waste; generation of
hazardous materials through improper handling of fuels lubricants or concrete additives; and waste
generation through inadequate disposal of excavated materials and demolition waste.

Poorly rehabilitated and designed canals may cause erosion and sedimentation during the operational
phase. Through increased agricultural activities, there may be agrochemical runoff through increased
fertilizers and pesticides; and water depletion through excessive pumping from rivers and aquifers.
Further, poor siting of dams or haffirs may disrupt the natural drainage and reduce downstream water
flows or cause flooding. There are potential impacts of dams and haffirs on biodiversity, such as ecosystem
disturbance through temporary dewatering or altered river flow that affects aquatic life, fish migration
and water quality; and other impacts on terrestrial and aquatic species. The activities in relation to dam
safety may pose risks to local ecosystems and water resources if activities involving large dam
rehabilitation, and small dam construction is not carefully planned and implemented.

The identified social risks and impacts include those related to labor and working conditions, such as the
use of forced labor or child labor, as well as the general risk of non-compliance with labor and working
conditions. Occupational Health and Safety (OHS) risks relate to risks of excavation, noise and vibration of
machinery, demolition, handling of solar panels, electrical works and lifting of heavy materials. There may
be a general risk in relation to a lack of understanding of EHS issues and injuries and accidents resulting
from that. Community Health and Safety risks include SEA/SH between workers and between workers and
community members, as well as security-related risks in conflict areas. Furthermore, the Project activities
can both trigger water access conflicts among community members. Women can be excluded from water
ownership and governance structures, and discriminatory practices may prevent access of certain groups
to project services and benefits. Activities may cause disruptions of livelihoods for farmers, fishers or
downstream river users. Social tension can occur based on works scheduling that interrups irrigation
during critical periods.

The Project components may bring risks of land acquisition and involuntary resettlement, as irrigation
canals, dam or haffir sites may require private land, or land use for canals, storages and ancillary works
can lead to temporary or permanent economic displacement. Project areas may also include communities
under ESS7, SSAHUTLCs, who may be disadvantaged through different concepts of land and water usage.

The ESMF lays out the E&S management process of the Project. The E&S risk management procedures will
be implemented through the sub-project selection process in Components 1-4. The procedures include an
assessment and analysis stage during which an E&S eligibility check against the Project exclusion list and
an E&S screening of the proposed sub-project will be conducted. The screening will help identify and
assess potential E&S risks, identify potentially sensitive E&S receptors likely to be negatively impacted. It
will stipulate the collection of E&S information of the sub-project site, including disaggregated data on
gender , age livelihood, ethnicity and vulnerability to ensure future measures are inclusive. It will reuire
the analysis of social power dynamics and institutional capacity to support empowerment and local
ownership.

Screening will help assign an E&S risk rating to each activity / sub-project. This will allow the identification
of appropriate mitigation measures for the sub-project, as well as the relevant E&S instruments required.
The E&S Screening Form and the collected baseline information in conjunction with the Generic ESMP (see
Annex 2) will indicate which mitigation measures and plans that may be relevant for the specific activities.
This can include existing Project-level procedures and plans, such as the Labor Management Procedures


                                                                                                              7
                                                                                                            Official Use Only
(LMP), Chance Find Procedures, Sexual Exploitation and Abuse (SEA) and Sexual Harassment (SH) Action
Plan, the Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP), Waste Management Plan (WMP), or the Integrated Pest
Management Framework (IPMF). Additional sub-project specific plans may have to be prepared, including:
For high and substantial risk sub-projects ESIAs and ESMPs will be prepared (see TOR and outlines in Annex
11). For moderate risk subprojects ESMPs will be prepared. Where required, Resettlement Action Plans
(RAPs) and/or Integrated Pest Management Plans (IPMP) will be prepared.

During the sub-project formulation and planning stage, planning for E&S risk mitigation will be undertaken
through adaptation of the relevant E&S procedures and plans. If site-specific E&S instruments are
necessary, the responsible party will prepare these and other applicable documents as needed ESIAs and
ESMPs will be prepared in line with para 13 and 14 of ESS1 of the World Bank’s ESF (‘indicative outline of
ESIA/ESMP’) and the project-specific requirements outlined in the Environmental and Social Commitment
Plan (ESCP) of this Project. Stakeholder consultations, including with identified vulnerable groups, will be
conducted as part of the E&S screening and the preparation of ESIAs/ESMPs – as laid out in the SEP – and
shall identify any E&S related concerns from project-affected parties.

All site-specific instruments will be submitted to the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) for clearance.
Furthermore, all documentation, permits and clearances under the Government’s environmental
regulations will be obtained, and the responsible staff for the implementation and monitoring of the E&S
instruments will be trained. Relevant E&S procedures and plans will be incorporated into contractor
bidding documents; and contractors trained on relevant procedures and plans.

The World Bank requires that the ESIAs/ESMPs are made available to project-affected groups, as defined
in the SEP, and to the public at large. A summary version will be translated into the main local languages
and disclosed to the public. The proposer will disseminate the ESIA or ESMPs at strategic locations and
offices of the ministries and according to the SEP. A Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) will be in place
for complaints on non-compliance with the disseminated documentation.

During the implementation and monitoring stage of sub-projects, the previously adopted E&S procedures
and site-specific plans will be implemented. Project teams will undertake regular site visits, receive regular
reporting from the field, track grievances and continue awareness raising of all implementers and
communities. The main monitoring and supervision responsibilities will sit with the PIU, which will
administer the overall project-related E&S monitoring and implementation as laid out in this ESMF. The
PIU will have overall responsibility for the implementation of the E&S mitigation measures, as well as for
monitoring for compliance. The E&S Specialists in the PIU will handle all monitoring, inspection and
reporting aspects. The contractor will be required to report to the responsible party on a monthly basis.
The PIU will receive all reporting and will provide general reporting as part of the Project Progress Report
to the World Bank, on a quarterly basis.

In the review and evaluation stage, the E&S procedures and plans will be completed and it will be assessed
whether plans have been effectively implemented and ensured that sites are properly restored.
Monitoring indicators will include sustainability ratings and community-level satisfaction with the sub-
project.

The Project will be implemented by the MILLS through a PIU. The National PIU will will provide oversight
for the sub projects under component 1. Further, it will coordinate with the regional PIUs for the
implementation of Component 2, and with the MoWE on the implementation of Component 3, and with
the MoA on the implementation of Component 4. The National PIU which will include a functional


                                                                                                            8
                                                                                                          Official Use Only
environmental and social risk management organizational structure. It will consist of one Environmental,
one Social Specialist and one OHS Specialist. They will be full time project staff. The regional PIU with
environmental and social experts will be a key implementer of Component 2, under the oversight of the
MILLS National PIU. For Component 3, the National PIU directly and the MoWE will act as key proposers
of sub-projects. The ESMF includes a capacity building plan stipulating training on various relevant E&S
topics for all project implementers.

A Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) will support the implementation of the Project. This Project GRM
facilitates the project to respond to concerns and grievances of the project-affected parties related to the
environmental and social performance of the project. The Project will provide mechanisms to receive and
facilitate resolutions to such concerns. The goal of the GRM is to strengthen accountability to beneficiaries
and to provide channels for project stakeholders to provide feedback and/or express grievances related to
project supported activities.

The estimated budget for the implementation of this ESMF is USD 2,540,000.

                                                                                                         Official Use Only
    1. Introduction
The proposed World Bank-financed Climate Resilient Irrigation for Sustainable Production (CRIPS) Project
will support the preparation and implementation of the NIP in Ethiopia, transitioning the sector from the
project-based approach to a more comprehensive, program-based model. It is expected that the NIP will
be developed and launched within the first years of CRISP implementation, and then further implemented
and operationalized during the remaining implementation period, ensuring that the NIP will serve as a
practical, implementable program, rather than a high-level strategy.

The PDO is to improve access to resilient irrigation services for sustainable production in Ethiopia, which
should be achieved through the following components; Component 1: Irrigation Rehabilitation and
Modernization; Component 2: Small-Scale Irrigation Development; Component 3: Dam Safety and
Irrigation Storage Improvement; and Component 4: Irrigation Service Improvement. The Project will be
implemented mainly by the MILLs at federal level with regional irrigation bureaus and collaborating
agencies (MoWE for dams/storage; MoA for value chains).

There are three prioritized irrigation schemes under Component 1 (Gode, Gidabo, and Ada’a Becho), for
which Environmental and Social Impact Assessments will be prepared. Screening modalities and TOR for
the ESIAs have been developed for this purpose. For the Technical Assistance (TA) activities of the Project
that relate to the national irrigation program and policies and regulations on irrigation water management,
a Strategic Environmental and Social Assessment (SESA) will be prepared. A Stakeholder Engagement Plan
(SEP) for the entire Project has been prepared as a separate document to ensure broad and inclusive
consultations with all Project stakeholders. In addition a Security Risk Assessment and Plan (SRAMP) will
also be prepared for the Project, not later than 3 months after effectiveness. For the dam safety measure
activities, Dam Safety Plans will be prepared by a panel of experts for relevant dams and water storage
structures of the Project. An institutional Environmental and Social Risk Management (ESRM) Capacity
Assessment will be conducted for the development of Component 3 activities.

For activities under Component 2, involving multiple new and rehabilitation of small-scale irrigation as
well as other components, this Environmental and Social Management Framework (ESMF) will continue
to Provide guidance on E&S riks managemn Procedure. This ESMF includes Labor Management
Procedures (LMP) a Sexual Exploitations and Abuse and Sexual Harassment (SEA/SH) Action Plan, a
Resettlement Framework (RF), a Waste Management Plan (WMP) and an Integrated Pest Management
Plan (IPMP) which will be relevant and applicable to the Project componets.

This Environmental and Social Management Framework (ESMF) has been developed as the key E&S
instrument to examine the risks and impacts as the Project consists of a series of sub-projects for which
risks and impacts cannot be determined until their details have been identified. It sets out the principles,
rules, guidelines and procedures to assess the risks and impacts of the project. It presents measures and
plans to reduce, mitigate and/or offset adverse risks and impacts, provisions for estimating and budgeting
the costs of these measures, and institutional arrangements and capacities for the implementation of the
E&S measures. It further includes E&S information on the Project area and assesses risks and impacts that
may occur and mitigation measures that might be expected to be used. The ESMF further establishes the
screening processes and tools as well as exclusion criteria for specific activities - to be directly
implemented by the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) in the MILLS in assessing the risks and impacts of
the sub-projects or activity. This will facilitate the recommendation of appropriate mitigation and
monitoring measures for each sub-project, including the preparation of site-specific Environmental and
Social Management Plans (ESMPs) and ESIAs where required.


                                                                                                         10
                                                                                                        Official Use Only
    2. Project Description
Project Development Objective (PDO): The PDO is to improve access to resilient irrigation services for
sustainable production in Ethiopia.

Project Components: Component 1: Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization. Component 1 will
support the rehabilitation and modernization of existing federal-level large- and medium-scale irrigation
schemes. Comprehensive screening and prioritization criteria were co-developed. Four irrigation
schemes—Gode, Gidabo, Ada’a Becho, Amibara and Sebeta Hawas—have been prioritized, each at a
different stage of readiness. At least two of these schemes have recent feasibility studies and detailed
designs, which will be advance. In addition, Component 1 will support the development of the Investment
Plan by financing the necessary studies and designs for pipeline projects. Component 1 will be primarily
led and implemented by MILLS. Key activities include: Irrigation Rehabilitation and Extension; Irrigation
Modernization; and Investment Plan Preparation.

Component 2: Small-Scale Irrigation Development. Component 2 will support the rehabilitation and
modernization of existing Small-Scale Irrigation (SSI), as well as the development of new SSI. Component
2 will also support the development of the Investment Plan by financing the necessary studies and designs
for pipeline SSIs. Component 2 will be led and implemented by the regional governments across all 13
regions of Ethiopia. Key activities include: rehabilitation and modernization of existing SSIs; and
development of New SSIs.

Component 3: Dam Safety and Irrigation Storage Improvement. Component 3 will support dam safety
activities for relevant dams, as well as the rehabilitation and development of small storages. It will
strengthen the institutional arrangements and enhance the dam safety capacity of both MILLs and MoWE.
Key activities include: dam safety assessment and measures; small storages; dam safety capacity building.

Component 4: Irrigation Service Improvement. At national level, Component 4 will improve the enabling
environment for sustainable irrigation development. Key activities include: support to Policy, Institution,
and Regulation (PIR); support to the development of the National Irrigation Program (NIP); support ot the
development of the National Irrigation Information System (NIIS); development of Quality Assurance
System; improvement of Operations & Maintenance (O&M); TA to pilot irrigation water resources
management mechanisms; and strengthen agricultural value chains linked to the project’s irrigation
investments.

Component 5: Project Management and Capacity Building, including improving Sector E&S Governance,
Institutional Capacity, and Risk/Impact Management.

The project will operate across diverse agro-ecological zones and in a context with varying levels of fragility,
conflict, and violence (FCV), including regions such as Amhara, Oromia, Benishangul-Gumuz, Afar, Somali,
and Tigray, where access constraints may affect stakeholder engagement, supervision, and security
management for field operations.




                                                                                                             11
                                                                                                            Official Use Only
    3. Legal, Regulatory and Institutional Framework
For substantial and high risk projects, the World Bank’s ESF applies in full. National E&S laws and
regulations are also followed, but the stricter standard prevails. This Project is rated substantial.


3.1 Legal and Policy Framework
The 1995 Constitution of the Federal Democratic Republic of Ethiopia considers environmental and social
impacts while implementing development projects. The Constitution is the basis for policies and strategies
relevant to social development, environmental protection and economic growth, in Articles 43, 44 and 92
and articles 40, 41, 42, 89 and 90, which refer to environmental and social issues respectively. It stipulates
sustainable development, environmental rights, and environmental objectives in Articles 43, 44, and 92
respectively.

Article 43: The Right to Development: Stipulates the right to improved living standards and to sustainable
development. Citizens have the right to participate in national development and, in particular, to be
consulted with respect to policies and projects affecting their community. The basic aim of development
activities shall be to enhance the capacity of citizens for development and to meet their basic needs.

Article 44: Environmental Rights: Stipulates the right to live in a clean and healthy environment. All
persons who have been displaced or whose livelihoods have been adversely affected as a result of state
programs have the right to commensurate monetary or alternative means of compensation, including
relocation with adequate state assistance.

Article 92: Environmental Objectives: The Government shall ensure that all Ethiopians live in a clean and
healthy environment. The design and implementation development initiatives shall not damage or destroy
the environment. People have the right to full consultation and to the expression of views in the planning
and implementations of environmental policies and projects that affect them directly. Government and
citizens shall have the duty to protect the environment. Moreover, the Article provides a basic outline to
safeguard the rights to thought, opinion and expression; and the rights of vulnerable groups that need
protection under Articles 29, 35, 36 and 41 respectively.

Article 35: Rights of Women: Women shall, in the enjoyment of rights and protection provided for by the
Constitution, have equal right with men. Women have the right to full consultation in the formulation of
national development policies, the designing and execution of projects, and particularly in the case of
projects affecting the interests of women. Women have the right to acquire, administer, control, use and
transfer property. In particular, they have equal rights with men with respect to use, transfer,
administration and control of land. They shall also enjoy equal treatment in the inheritance of property.

Article 36: Right of Children: Every child has the right not to be subjected to exploitative practices, neither
to be required nor permitted to perform work which may be hazardous or harmful to his or her education,
health or well-being.

Article 41: Economic, Social and Cultural Rights: The State shall, within available means, allocate resources
to provide rehabilitation and assistance to the physically and mentally disabled, the aged, and to children
who are left without parents or guardian. The State shall pursue policies which aim to expand job




                                                                                                            12
                                                                                                           Official Use Only
opportunities for the unemployed and the poor and shall accordingly undertake programs and public
works projects.

Article 40(3): recognizes land as a common property of the Nations, Nationalities, and Peoples of
Ethiopia and prohibits sale or any other exchange of land. It recognizes that the use rights over “holding
land” are open and no time limit on this usufruct as long as the subject to a proof of permanent physical
property, ability to farm continuously and meet administrative dues and obligations.

Article 26: protects against unlawful seizure of property, stating “Everyone shall have the right to his
privacy and physical integrity. This right shall include protection from explorations of his person, his
home, his property and protection from appropriation of property under his possession”.

Environmental Protection Organs Establishment Proclamation No. 295/2002: establishes a system that
fosters coordinated but differentiated responsibilities among environmental protection agencies at federal
and regional levelsas well as sector environmental units so as to foster sustainable use of environmental
resources, thereby avoiding possible conflicts of interests and duplication of efforts.

Environmental Impact Assessment Proclamation No. 299/2002: This Proclamation harmonizes and
integrates environmental, economic, cultural,and social considerations for decision making processes in a
manner that promotes sustainable development. It stipulates the requirement to prepare EIAs, outlines
how to prepare them, and to whom the EIA report is submitted. It further promulgates the establishment
of environmental protection organs. The proclamation asserts the organizational requirements and the
need to establish systems that enablescoordination but with different responsibilities of environmental
protection agencies at federal and regional levels. The EIAs must cover social and ecological effects in
regard to resettlement, biodiversity loss, water quality and sedimentation. No dam construction permit
can be issued without EIA approval from the Environment, Forest and Climate Change Commission
(EFCCC).

Ethiopian Water Resources Management Proclamation, No. 197/2000: ensures that the water resources
of the country are protected and utilized for the highest social and economic benefits of the people of
Ethiopia, to follow up and supervise that they are duly conserved, ensure that harmful effects of water are
prevented, and that the management of water resources is carried out properly. It proclaims that all water
resources of the country are the common property of the Ethiopian people and the state. The
Proclamation includes large hydropower and irrigation dams. It regulates permit requirements for dams;
that all dam projects must align with basin master plans and integrated water resource management
principles; that the Ministry can set technical standards for dam design, construction and operation; and
that domestic and environmental flows must be safeguarded even when building dams.

Ethiopian Water Resources Management Regulations – Council of Ministers Regulation No. 115/2005:
implements the 2000 Proclamation by detailing the operational rules for water resources management. It
governs water abstraction, construction of waterworks, discharge permits, regulation of pollution, and
enforcement procedures. It sets groundwater protections, authorizes drilling safeguards, and defines
inspection regimes. It permits formation of water users’ cooperatives, including for irrigation and their
registration and federal or regional levels. Dam-related rules include that water abstraction and storage
require licensing. The regulation further sets inspection powers for dam construction sites and governs
pollution prevention in reservoirs.




                                                                                                        13
                                                                                                       Official Use Only
Irrigation Water Users’ Associations – Proclamation No. 841/2014: establishes the legal basis for forming
and governing irrigation water user associations (IWUAs). It defines how associations are formed,
registered and organized and managed. It clarifies roles, responsibilities, rights and duties of members and
supervisory bodies. It sets mechanisms for dispute resolution and service agreements for irrigation
provision.

Irrigation Development Investment Incentives – Council of Ministers regulation No. 162/2009: Provides
incentives to encourage private investment in irrigation projects over 50 hectares.

River Basin Councils and Authorities Proclamation No. 534/2007: manages dams at river basin scale.
Basin authorities coordinate between dam developers, irrigation users and environmental agencies. The
proclamation encourages multipurpose use f dams (e.g. hydropower, irrigation and flood control).

Environmental Pollution Control Proclamation No. 300/2002: requires pollution control mechanisms in
development projects before they are established and made operational. It requires development projects
to execute continuous activities that mitigate environmental pollution. Thus, environmental audit or
inspection shall be made undertaken on the activities of development projects to evaluate the compliance
of their pollution mitigation strategy with the standards and regulations of the country.

Fertilizer Manufacturing and Trade Proclamation No. 137/1998: governs the production, trade and
regulation of fertilizers for agricultural productivity.

Pesticide Registration and Control Proclamation No. 674/2010: Sets the legal framework for registering,
distributing and using pesticides safely.

Solid Waste Management Proclamation No. 513/2007: promotes community participation in order to
overcome the adverse effects from the disposal of wastes and to enhance benefits resulting from solid
wastes. The provisions require the preparation of solid waste management action plans by respective
urban local governments and project activities that produce solid waste.

Hazardous Waste Management and Disposal Control Proclamation Proc No.1090/2018: creates a system
for the environmentally sound management and disposal of hazardous waste; and to prevent the damage
to the human or animal health, the environment, biodiversity and property due to the mismanagement of
hazardous waste.

Public Health Proclamation No.200/2000: forbids the discharge of untreated liquid waste generated from
septic tanks,seepage pits and industries into water bodies, or water convergences. It also prohibits the
disposal ofsolid or liquid or any other waste in a way which contaminates the environment or affects public
health.

The Labor Proclamation No. 1156/2019: stipulates conducive working environments for the
laborers/workers in Ethiopia. The Proclamation bestowed responsibility upon the employer for the
creation of good working environment for the employees. The employer will take all the necessary
measures to safeguard the health and safety of the workers. It also intends to form durable industrial
peace, sustainable productivity and competitiveness that will contribute to the overall development of the
country.




                                                                                                         14
                                                                                                        Official Use Only
Proclamation No. 1161/2019: Expropriation of Land for Public Purposes, Payments of Compensation
and Resettlement of Displaced People: The Proclamation gives priority rights to develop land for the
landholders when the capacity of the landholders to develop the land as per the approved land use plan;
urban structural plan; or development master plan is presented. It states, “landholders whose holdings
are within the area prescribed to be redeveloped shall have priority rights to develop theirlands according
to the plan either individually or in a group” (Article 7, sub-article 1-2).

Gender Based Violence Proclamation No. 1097/2018: bestows powers and duties to the Ministry of
Women, Children and Youth to: “design strategies to effectively preventand take measures against gender-
based violence against women; implement same in collaboration with relevant organs; facilitate the
setting up centers for provision of holistic health, psychological, legal and rehabilitation services for
women who were victims of violence; and follow up the implementation of same.”

Proclamation No. 89/1997 confirms and details the Constitutional principle that holding rights on land
can be assigned to peasants and pastoralists. Ethiopian peasants have the right to obtain land without
payment and the protection against eviction from their possession. Ethiopian pastoralists have the right
to free land for grazing and cultivation as well as the right not to be displaced from their own lands.

Proclamation No.1161/2019 on “Expropriation of Land for Public Purposes, Payments of Compensation
and Resettlement of Displaced People” gives priority rights to develop Land for the Landholders when the
capacity of the Landholders to develop the land as per the approved land use plan; urban structural plan;
or development master plan is presented. It states, “Landholders whose holdings are within the area
prescribed to be redeveloped shall have priority rights to develop their lands according to the plan either
individually or in a group” (Article 7, sub-article 1-2).

Council of Ministers Regulation No. 472/2020: contains property valuation and compensation methods
and formulae that should be used in calculating compensation for various properties. It also contains lump
sum compensation to be paid for severed social relationship and moral damages.

Environmental Policy of Ethiopia (1997): The purpose of the Policy is to improve and enhance the health
and qualityof life of all Ethiopians through promoting sustainable social and economic development. This
shall be achieved through sound management and use of resources and the environment as a whole so as
to meet the needs of the present generation without compromising the ability of future generations to
meet their own needs. With the intention of having sustainable environmental management, it
encourages the creation of organizational and institutional frameworks from federal to community levels.

Ethiopian Water Resources Management Policy (1999): enhances and promotes all national efforts
towards the efficient, equitable and optimum utilization of the available Water Resources of Ethiopia for
significant socioeconomic development on sustainable basis. The Policy aims to ensure access to water for
everyone fairly and in a sustainable manner, protect water resources and sources, and promote
cooperation for the management of river basins. The Policy also requires water resources schemes and
projects to have Environmental Impact Assessment and Evaluation.

The National Biodiversity Strategy and Action plan (NBSAP) for 2015-2020: stipulates biodiversity
conservation, sustainable use, and equitable benefit sharing. It establishes guiding principles, national
targets, and institutional riles to conserve biodiversity. It strengthens protected areas, promotes
conservation, integrates biodiversity in development strategies and ensures equitable sharing of benefits
from genetic resources.


                                                                                                        15
                                                                                                       Official Use Only
Dam Safety & Technical Guidelines: Ethiopia has dam safety manuals and technical standards developed
by the Ministry of Water and Energy. They guide on design flood requirements; seismic safety; emergency
action plans; and regular inspection and maintenance regimes.

The National Social Protection Policy (NSPP) (2012): envisions “to see all Ethiopians enjoy social and
economic wellbeing, security and social justice “and recognizes the contribution of social protection to the
development goals of the country. Overall, the policy commits the government to move beyond the partial,
and fragmented, provision of Social Protection to establish a comprehensive SocialProtection system.

National Policy on Women (1993): With the announcement of the National Policy of Women in 1993 and
the promulgation of the new Constitution in 1995, the Ethiopian Government declared its commitment to
the equitable socio-economic development of women. It aims to institutionalize the political, economic
and social rights of women by creating appropriate structures in government offices and institutions so
that public policies and interventions become gender-responsive in order to ensure equitable
development for all Ethiopians.

Environmental and Social Impact Assessment Guidelines and Directives: The Environmental Protection
Authority has provided comprehensive guidelines on “Environmental and Social Impact Assessments
Guideline September 2017”. The ESIA guideline document prepared in 2000 provides essential information
covering the following elements: Environmental assessment and management in Ethiopia, ESIA Process,
Standards and Guidelines, and Sectorial Environmental impact assessments in Ethiopia covering
agriculture, industry, transport, mining, dams and reservoirs, tanneries, textiles, hydropower generation,
irrigation projects and resettlement. The Environmental and Social Management Plan Preparation
Guidelines from 2004 outline the content that needs to be featured while preparing an ESMP and provides
template forms to be used for such purposes. The guideline also provides guidance on the preparation of
institutional arrangements for implementation of ESMPs. The ESIA Guidelines from 2017 outline a revised
version of the Ethiopian ESIA Guidelines with the aim to integrate social issues (gender, health, and
workplace rights) into to the environmental impact assessment system of the country.

In addition, a the Directive No. 1/2008 determines projects to identify and list those investment projects
subject to mandatory ESIA. The regions are entitled to issue similar directive to their own specific cases
based on these directives. Extensive lists of project types requiring ESIA are provided in this directive.

This guideline outlines the screening, review and approval process for development projects in Ethiopia
and defines the criteria for undertaking an ESIA. Projects are categorized into three schedules:
    ▪ Schedule 1: Projects which may have adverse and significant environmental impacts and require a
        full ESIA.
    ▪ Schedule 2: Projects, whose type, scale or other relevant characteristics have potential to cause
        some significant environmental impacts, but are not likely to warrant a full ESIA.
    ▪ Schedule 3: Projects which would have no significant environmental and social impact and do not
        require an ESIA.

However, projects situated in an environmentally sensitive areas, such as land prone to erosion;
desertification; areas of historic or archaeological interest; important landscape; religiously important
area, etc… will fall under Schedule I irrespective of the nature of the project.

3.3 World Bank Environmental and Social Standards (ESS)
The Environmental and Social Framework (ESF) sets out the World Bank’s commitment to sustainable
development through a set of Environmental and Social Standards (ESS) that are designed to support
borrowers’ projects with the aim of ending extreme poverty and promoting shared prosperity.

This section provides a summary of the ESS relevant for this Project.

ESS1: Assessment and Management of Environmental and Social Risks and Impacts: sets out the
borrower’s responsibilities for assessing, managing and monitoring environmental and social risks and
impacts associated with each stage of a project supported by the Bank through Investment Project
Financing, in order to achieve environmental and social outcomes consistent with the ESS. The Project is
subject to ESS1 and will follow it through during preparation, design and implementation.

ESS2: Labor and Working Conditions. ESS2 recognizes the importance of employment creation and
income generation in the pursuit of poverty reduction and inclusive economic growth. Borrowers can
promote sound worker-management relationships and enhance the development benefits of a project by
treating workers in the project fairly and providing safe and healthy working conditions.

ESS3: Resource Efficiency and Pollution Prevention and Management. ESS3 recognizes that economic
activity and urbanization often generate pollution to air, water and land, and consume finite resources that
may threaten people, ecosystem services and the environment at the local, regional and global levels. This
ESS sets out the requirements to address resource efficiency and pollution prevention and management
throughout the project life cycle consistent with GIIP. The Project considers resource efficiency related to
all irrigation activities as part of this ESMF and site-specific E&S instruments.




                                                                                                         17
                                                                                                        Official Use Only
ESS4: Community Health and Safety. ESS4 recognizes that project activities, equipment and infrastructure
can increase community exposure to risks and impacts. In addition, communities that are already
subjected to impacts from climate change may also experience an acceleration or intensification of
impacts due to project activities.ESS4 addresses the health, safety, and security risks and impacts on
project-affected communities and the corresponding responsibility of borrowers to avoid or minimize such
risks and impacts, with particular attention to people who, because of their particular circumstances, may
be vulnerable. While not explicitly mentioned, prevention and mitigation of different forms of gender-
based violence, specifically SEA/SH, is covered by ESS4.

ESS4 further covers the safety of dams. It applies to the design, construction, operation and maintenance
of new and existing dams., as well as structures that rely on dams (reservoirs, irrigation schemes, flood
control and water supply systems). The Borrower must take measures to ensure that dam failure or
operational failure risks are minimized through proper design, construction, supervision, quality
assurance, and regular safety inspections. It requires independent dam safety experts for large dams (15
meters high or of special complexity). It further covers appurtenant structures, including dikes,
embankments, spillways, tunnels and other components.

ESS5: Land Acquisition, Restrictions on Land Use and Involuntary Resettlement. ESS5 recognizes that
project-related land acquisition and restrictions on land use can have adverse impacts on communities
and persons. Project-related land acquisition or restrictions on land use may cause physical displacement
(relocation, loss of residential land or loss of shelter), economic displacement (loss of land, assets or access
to assets, leading to loss of income sources or other means of livelihood), or both. The term “involuntary
resettlement” refers to these impacts. Resettlement is considered involuntary when affected persons or
communities do not have the right to refuse land acquisition or restrictions on land use that result in
displacement. The Project considers these risks and impacts as part of this ESMF.

ESS6: Biodiversity Conservation and Sustainable Management of Living Natural Resources. ESS6
recognizes that protecting and conserving biodiversity and sustainably managing living natural resources
are fundamental to sustainable development. Biodiversity is defined as the variability among living
organisms from all sources including, inter alia, terrestrial, marine and other aquatic ecosystems and the
ecological complexes of which they are a part; this includes diversity within species, between species and
of ecosystems. Biodiversity often underpins ecosystem services valued by humans. Impacts on biodiversity
can therefore often adversely affect the delivery of ecosystem services. To manage these risks and impacts,
the ESMF includes E&S screening criteria for excluding subprojects that have potential adverse impacts on
ecologically sensitive areas and natural habitats; an indicative list of mitigation measures based on
potential risk assessment of the subprojects. Mitigation measures will be detailed in site-specific E&S
instruments.

ESS7: Indigenous Peoples/Sub-Saharan African Historically Underserved Traditional Local Communities.
This ESS applies to distinct social and cultural groups. ESS7 contributes to poverty reduction and
sustainable development by ensuring that projects supported by the Bank enhance opportunities for
SSAHUTLC to participate in, and benefit from, the development process in ways that do not threaten their
unique cultural identities and well-being. Some communities in the project area may meet the criteria for
SSAHUTLCs. The Project will screen sub-projects for the presence of SSAHUTLCs and ensure meaningful
and culturally appropriate consultations where they are present. Furthermore, sub-project ESIAs and
ESMPs will capture the risks and impacts in relation to SSAHUTLCs.




                                                                                                             18
                                                                                                            Official Use Only
ESS8: Cultural Heritage. ESS8 recognizes that cultural heritage provides continuity in tangible and
intangible forms between the past, present and future. People identify with cultural heritage as a
reflection and expression of their constantly evolving values, beliefs, knowledge and traditions. Cultural
heritage, in its many manifestations, is important as a source of valuable scientific and historical
information, as an economic and social asset for development, and as an integral part of people’s cultural
identity and practice. ESS8 sets out measures designed to protect cultural heritage throughout the project
life cycle. Given the planned construction under the Project, this ESS is applicable.

ESS10: Stakeholder Engagement and Information Disclosure: recognizes the importance of open and
transparent engagement between the Borrower and project stakeholders as an essential element of good
international practice. Effective stakeholder engagement can improve the environmental and social
sustainability of projects, enhance project acceptance and make a significant contribution to successful
project design and implementation. The borrower will engage with stakeholders throughout the project
life cycle, commencing such engagement as early as possible in the project development process and in a
timeframe that enables meaningful consultations with stakeholders on project design. The nature, scope
and frequency of stakeholder engagement will be proportionate to the nature and scale of the project and
its potential risks and impacts. The borrower has prepared a Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP)
proportionate to the nature and scale of the project and its potential risks and impacts.

For a gap analysis between the national legislation and these ESSs, see Annex 9.

3.4 Health and Safety (EHS) Guidelines
The general WB Environmental, Health and Safety Guidelines (EHSG) will serve as a technical
reference for the Project activities together with the relevant Industry Sector EHS Guidelines, which
provide guidance to users or project implementers on EHS issues in specific industry sectors. It
includes environmental guideline on air emissions and ambient air quality, energy conservation,
waste water and water quality, water conservation, noise and contaminated land. The occupational
health and safety guideline includes hazards related to chemicals, physical and biological that cause
health and safety risks. The community health and safety guideline include traffic safety, structural,
life and fire, water quality and diseases. The WBG General EHS Guidelines provide guideline values
on air quality, wastewater, and noise emission, which are applicable during most of the subprojects
implementation and operation. Ethiopian EHS regulatory requirements applicable to the project
include the following: The Ethiopian EIA Guideline (2000) provides guideline values for water and air
quality; Ethiopia drinking water standard published by the Ethiopian Standard Agency (2013) provides
guideline values (limits) on physical, chemical, and bacteriological water quality. The standard also
provides sampling frequency for water quality testing of water supply schemes/systems.
Reconstruction of existing and construction of new WASH facilities are required to satisfy thisstandard
for potable water supply. The EHS requirements for emissions control are usually addressed through
the environmental and social assessment and monitoring process while building designs require
permits from the concerned local authorities.


    4. Identification and Assessment of Potential Environmental and
       Social Risks and Impacts
The environmental and social risk classification for the project is Substantial.



                                                                                                          19
                                                                                                          Official Use Only
Environmental risk and impacts: The project’s environmental risk rating is Substantial at this stage. The
environmental risks and impacts of the Project activities are mainly associated with activities under
Component 1, rehabiliation of large and medium scale irrgation, 2, the rehabilitation or construction of
SSI, and Component 3 the rehabilitation of dams and the construction of small dams and haffirs. The
rehabilitation and modernization of irrigation schemes could lead to altered hydrological regimes,
affecting downstream water availability and potentially impacting aquatic ecosystems.

Some Components’ activities include construction-related risks and impacts, such as air pollution and dust
emission during the works; soil and water contamination and degradation of water bodies through the
discharge of waste; pollution of local surface water sources; generation of domestic and construction
waste; generation of hazardous materials through improper handling of fuels lubricants or concrete
additives; and waste generation through inadequate disposal of excavated materials and demolition
waste.

Solar panels in Component 2 may release emissions associated with solar energy, such as heavy metals
like lead, mercury, and cadmium. Used panels may generate hazardous waste through improper recycling,
and generally, discarded solar panels generate e-waste.

Poorly designed canals in Component 1 and 2 may cause erosion and sedimentation during the operational
phase. Through increased agricultural activities, there may be agrochemical runoff through increased
fertilizers and pesticides; and water depletion through excessive pumping from rivers and aquifers. can
result in downstream impacts due to the potential for structure failure associated with aging
infrastructure, poor design, inadequate maintenance, or substandard materials can increase the risk of
collapse or malfunction; unplanned releases or failures can cause flooding, threatening lives, property,
agriculture, and natural habitats downstream. Evaporization from irrigated fields leaving behind salts may
cause soil degradation and reduce soil fertility. The use of agrochemicals (fertilizers, pesticides) associated
with intensified irrigated agriculture could result in water and soil pollution, affecting both human and
ecological health.

In Component 3, poor siting of dams or haffirs may disrupt the natural drainage and reduce downstream
water flows or cause flooding. Construction and rehabilitation works may cause soil disturbance and
erosion through earthworks for embankments, trenches and ponds. In the operational phase, water in the
reservoir can be contaminated through poor farming practices or open defecation upstream; there can be
flooding and structural destruction and erosion through embankment failure; and poor water quality
through livestock watering and agrochemical use. Potential impacts of dams and haffirs on biodiversity
include ecosystem disturbance through temporary dewatering or altered river flow that affects aquatic
life, fish migration and water quality; and other impacts on terrestrial and aquatic species. The activities
in relation to dam safety may pose risks to local ecosystems and water resources if activities involving large
dam rehabilitation, and small dam construction is not carefully planned and implemented.

The planned TA under Component 4, such as preparation of pipeline investment projects and development
of a NIP, may result in downstream EHS risks and impacts.

The social risk rating of the Project is substantial. The proposed project has positive social impacts.
Generally, the project targets improved irrigation for farmers and with that aims to improve livelihoods, as
well as enhances community safety through dam safety initiatives.




                                                                                                            20
                                                                                                           Official Use Only
However, the Project also has adverse social risks and impacts. These are related to ESS2, Labor and
Workind Conditions, since the Project will trigger a large amount of small-scale construction and
rehabilitation in Component 2 and 3. As such it includes general ESS2 risks, such as the use of forced labor
or child labor, as well as the general risk of non-compliance with labor and working conditions. OHS risks
relate to risks of excavation, noise and vibration of machinery, demolition, handling of solar panels,
electrical works and lifting of heavy materials. There may be a general risk in relation to a lack of
understanding of EHS issues and injuries and accidents resulting from that. There are risks of inadequate
Personal Protective Equipment (PPE) and inadequate supervision of safety protocols during rehabilitation
works on dams, and the risk of lack of access to workers’ GRM – especially given uneven contractor
compliance in rural areas. Risks related to labor influx are minor, given that Component 2 and 3 include
mostly small construction works.

Community Health and Safety risks under ESS4 include SEA/SH between workers and between workers
and community members, as well as security-related risks in conflict areas. Improper waste disposal during
construction works can have health implications for community members, and there are community safety
risks during works in the vicinity of project works, including through construction traffic near settlements.
Solar panels, under Component 2, can have impacts on human health through mold or contact with
electrical outlets. Stagnant water in poorly managed systems can pose communityh health risks, including
through posing breeding grounds for mosqitos. Workers may pose risks for the communities as they may
bring communicable diseases. Construction works bring traffic of heavy vehicles, posing safety risks for
community members. Open water sites bear risks of drowning for commuinty members, eespecially
children, as well as livestock.

The Project activities can both trigger water access conflicts among community members. Women can be
excluded from water ownership and governance structures, and discriminatory practices may prevent
access of certain groups to project services and benefits. There may be ethnic tension triggered among
project workers and between project woerkers and communities. Activities may cause disruptions of
livelihoods for farmers, fishers or downstream river users. Social tension can occur based on works
scheduling that interrups irrigation during critical periods.

There are also risks of land acquisition and involuntary resettlement, as construction/ rehabilitation of
irrigation canals, dam or haffir sites may require private land, or land use for canals, storages and ancillary
works can lead to temporary or permanent economic displacement. Grievance procedues for such cases
may be incosistently applied. Furthermore, the activities may trigger conflict around land use and
ownership. Project areas may also include communities under ESS7, SSAHUTLCs, who may be
disadvantaged through different concepts of land and water usage. At the same time, there may be chance
finds during the works under both components, as well as irrigation structures or dams that are sited along
with sites or cultural or spiritual significance.

Inadequate stakeholder engagement, especially in hard to reach areas, and lack of access GRM are risks in
relation to ESS10. Inadequate stakeholder engagement in particular can lead to mistrust among local
communities. There is a risk of elite capture of project benefits and the exclusion of women, youth,
persons with disabilities, Internally Displaced people (IDPs), and SSAHUTLCs, who may be excluded from
benefits, decision-making or who may be asked to pay hefty fees for irrigation services.

For a further assessment of the risks, see Annex 2.

                                                                                                           Official Use Only

Stage 1: Sub-project assessment and analysis – E&S Screening:
All proposed activities or sub-project in Component 1-4 will be checked for their eligibility to ensure that
they are within the boundaries of the Project’s eligible activities, and they are not considered as activities
listed on the E&S Exclusion List (See Annex 1).

In a second step, all sub-projects and activities are screened for their E&S risks and impacts, including
poverty targeting, gender inclusion, and environmental and climate risks. For Component 2 sub-project
activities, the respective Regional Governments will conduct the E&S screening, done by the
Environmental and Social Specialists in the regional governments, in close coordination with the engineers
and other technical staff working on subproject planning and design. The Environmental Officer in the
Regional Government will review and approve the documents prior to submission to the PIU.

For Component 3 and 4 sub-projects, the proposer (e.g. MoWE or MoA) screen the sub-project in close
coordination with the engineers and other technical staff working on subproject planning and design. The
respective Ministry’s Environmental Officer will review the results prior to submission to the PIU.

Sub-projects site selection and screening must be based on community participation and consultation,
which must be documented. The E&S screening process involves: a) reconnaissance of the subproject
areas/routes and their surroundings, b) identification of the major subproject activities and c) preliminary
assessment of the impacts of these activities on the ecological and socio-economic environment of the
subproject surrounding areas.

       responses on the screening form (Annex 1), the measures taken to this effect should be described briefly.

It is expected that most rehabilitation sub-projects under Component 2 will require an ESMP, while sub-
projects under Component 1 and 3 may require and ESIA/ESMP or ESMP. For both components, the
identification of specific risks in view of ESS5 may require the additional preparation of a Resettlement
Action Plan (RAP) (see Section on Resettlement Framework below).

Local requirements: Article 5 of the EIA Proclamation includes directives on which projects a) require an
EIA, b) which projects do not require an EIA, and which may require a partial EIA or other evaluation. An
initial Environmental Examination (IEE) may be used in cases where it is not clear if a full EIA is required.
Projects are grouped in schedules:
     1. Schedule-1: Projects which may have adverse and significant environmental effects require a full
          EIA study (e.g. large dams, reservoirs, major water supply schemes, major infrastructure works)
     2. Schedule-2: Projects whole type, scale or characteristics mean they may cause some significant
          environmental impacts, but are less likely to need a full EIA (smaller infrastructure works, medium
          scale projects whose impacts may be significant depending on location, scale or characteristics)
     3. Schedule-3: Projects that are unlikely to have significant environmental impacts, and hence do not
          require an EIA (smaller projects with minimal E&S impacts, small-scale infrastructure, small scale
          irrigation, small water supply or drainage works in non-sensitive areas).

Proponent submits information, including on location, size, likely environmental and social impacts,
proposed mitigation measures and based on that the relevant agency decides whether a full EIA is
required, whether conditions or modifications are sufficient, or to reject the proposal. Any project in an
environmentally sensitive area (e.g. land prone to erosion or desertification, areas of historical or
archaeological importance, special landscapes, etc..) is treated more strictly.

Procedures, etc.) or develop relevant site-specific E&S instruments (e.g. ESIAs, ESMPs, RAPs, IPMPs, ect).

out in the SEP – and shall identify any E&S related concerns from project-affected parties.

Project Generic ESMP: The table matrix in Annex 2 contains a detailed Project Generic ESMP. It identifies
prevention, minimization, mitigation and compensation measures for each activity. The mitigation table
serves as a reference on potential risks and impacts, associated with international industry best practices,
mitigation measures and indicators or outcomes that can be planned and implemented throughout the
sub-project or project. The risks and impacts, mitigation measures and monitoring indicators are sorted
by ESS.




                                                                                                                26
                                                                                                               Official Use Only
Site-Specific Mitigation Measures: The Generic ESMP will be used as a basis to develop the site-specific
E&S mitigation measures for specific sub-projects, based on the E&S risk screening results. Each activity
takes place in a specific context and has specific characteristics, which will be considered in the
preparation of site-specific E&S mitigation measures, for example in ESIAs and/or site-specific ESMPs.
These sub-project instruments should be in place before any construction or rehabilitation activity
(including land clearance, contractor mobilization, etc.) take place. Depending on the level of anticipated
risks and impacts, other mitigation measures may be added, and monitoring mechanisms adjusted prior
to sub-project commencement.

Bidding Documents: The ESMPs shall be included in the procurement and contracting of contractors -
including bidding documents for potential civil works, as well as other WB standard EHS terms and
conditions for procurement and any subproject-specific requirements. Codes of Conduct (CoC) shall be
required to be signed by all workers of contractors, subcontractors, primary suppliers, and their workers
(see Annex 5).

Approvals and Disclosure: The contents of the site-specific E&S instruments will be shared with relevant
stakeholders in an accessible manner, and consultations will be held with the affected communities on the
environmental and social risks and mitigation measures. The site-specific E&S instruments will be
submitted to the PIU and will be subject to a no-objection from the PIU. The PIU will submit the first five
ESIAs, ESMPs, IPMP and every RAP to the World Bank for prior review and no objection. After this first 5,
the World Bank and the PIU will reassess whether prior review is needed for further E&S instruments.

The responsible party will also complete the documentation, permits and clearances required under the
government’s Environmental Regulation before any project activities begin. Once cleared by the World
Bank, the ESIAs/ESMPs and ESMPs will be submitted by the proposer to the Regional Environmental
Protection Authority for approval and licensing. The Authority will review the ESIA/ESMP and provide to
the proposer with written comments within fifteen (15) days of its receipt. The Authority will review the
ESIA/ESMP and notify to the proposer in writing if it is cleared. Where the ESIA/ESMP is found to be
inadequate, the Authority will return it to the proposer for revision, taking into consideration the
comments and objections of the Authority. No works can commence prior to approval and licensing by
the Authority.

The World Bank requires that the ESIAs/ESMPs are made available to project-affected groups, as defined
in the SEP, and to the public at large. A summary version will be translated into the main local languages
and disclosed to the public. The proposer will disseminate the ESIA or ESMPs at strategic locations and
offices of the ministries and according to the SEP. A Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) will be in place
for complaints on non-compliance with the disseminated documentation.

Capacity Building: At this stage, staff who will be working on the respective sub-project activities will be
trained in the specific E&S instruments relevant to the activities they work on. The responsible party
should provide such training to field staff. The responsible party should also ensure that all selected
contractors and subcontractors understand and incorporate E&S mitigation measures relevant to them as
standard operating procedures for civil works. The responsible party should provide training to selected
contractors to ensure that they understand and incorporate E&S mitigation measures; and plan for
cascading training to be delivered by contractors to subcontractors. The responsible party should further
ensure that the entities or communities responsible for ongoing operation and maintenance of the
investment have received training on operations stage E&S management measures as applicable (See
Section on Capacity building below).


                                                                                                          27
                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Staeg 3: Implementation and Monitoring – E&S Implementation:
Monitoring: The main monitoring and supervision responsibilities rest with the PIU, which will administer
the overall project-related E&S monitoring and implementation as laid out in this ESMF. The PIU will have
overall responsibility for the implementation of the E&S mitigation measures, as well as for monitoring for
compliance. The E&S Specialists and the OHS Specialist in the PIU will handle all monitoring, inspection
and reporting aspects.

The responsible party working to implement the project will ensure that monitoring practices include the
E&S risks identified in the ESMF and in the site-specific E&S instruments, where applicable, and will
monitor the implementation of E&S risk management mitigation plans as part of regular project
monitoring. For Component 1 and 2 activities, the regional PIU will be responsible for day-to-day
monitoring and inspection of E&S mitigation measures in relation to construction and rehabilitation of
irrigation schemes. E&S-related monitoring will focus on compliance by its contractors, sub-contractors
and suppliers. For Component 3, the proposer, MILLS or MoWE, will be responsible for day-to-day
inspections and monitoring, while the PIU will conduct overall monitoring of activities. The PIU E&S
Specialists will assess progress of activities against the ESCP, ESMF, and the SEP, and subsequent
ESIAs/ESMPs.

The responsible party will monitor and supervise the preparation of C-ESMPs by contractors and will be
responsible for the monitoring and supervision of contractors and sub-contractors and suppliers as
stipulated in the ESMP. If monitoring and supervision results in the identification of non-compliance by
contractors, the responsible party will report such to the PIU and will discuss and oversee the
implementation of corrective actions of the contractor.

Capacity Building: Throughout Project implementation, the responsible party will continue to provide
training and awareness raising to relevant stakeholders, such as staff, selected contractors, and
communities, to support the implementation of the E&S risk management mitigation measures. An initial
list of training needs is proposed below.

The responsible party and the PIU will also track grievances/beneficiary feedback in line with the SEP
during project implementation to use as a monitoring tool for implementation of project activities and
environmental and social mitigation measures.

Reporting: The contractor will be required to report to the responsible party on a monthly basis. The
responsible party will report to the PIU on a quarterly basis (see Annex 7). The PIU will receive all reporting
and will provide general reporting as part of the Project Progress Report to the World Bank, on a quarterly
basis. At a minimum, the reporting will include (i) the overall implementation of E&S risk management
instruments and measures, (ii) any environmental or social issues arising as a result of project activities
and how these issues will be remedied or mitigated, including timelines, (iii) Occupational Health and
Safety performance (including incidents and accidents), (iv) community health and safety, (v) stakeholder
engagement updates, in line with the SEP, (vi) public notification and communications, (vii) progress on
the implementation and completion of project works, and (viii) summary of grievances/beneficiary
feedback received, actions taken, and complaints closed out, in line with the SEP.

Incident and Accident Reporting: The World Bank will be notified promptly (within 48 hours) of any
incident or accident related to the Project which has, or is likely to have, a significant adverse effect on the
environment, communities, the public or workers, including, inter alia, cases of SEA/SH and accidents that


                                                                                                             28
                                                                                                            Official Use Only
result in death, serious or multiple injuries (by the PIU). The PIU will provide sufficient detail regarding the
scope, severity, and possible causes of the incident or accident, indicating immediate measures taken or
that are planned to be taken to address it. The report should also include any information provided by any
contractor or supervising entity. The report will be submitted through the relevant form in the World
Bank’s ESIRT.

All other incidents will be reported in the quarterly E&S report, with a Root Cause Analysis (RCA) prepared
by the contractor within 15 days of the incident or accident notification, which will be followed by the
detailed action plan. In cases where prolonged investigations are required, quarterly reports will be
provided.

Stage 4: Review and Evaluation – E&S Completion:
Upon completion of the sub-project activities, the responsible party will review and evaluate progress and
completion of project activities and all E&S mitigation measures. Especially for civil works, the responsible
party will monitor activities with regard to site restoration and landscaping in the affected areas to ensure
that the activities are done to an appropriate and acceptable standard before closing the contracts, in
accordance with measures identified in the site-specific E&S instrument and other plans. The sites must
be restored to at least the same condition and standard that existed prior to commencement of works.
Any pending issues must be resolved before a sub-project is considered fully completed. The responsible
party jointly with the PIU will prepare the completion report describing the final status of compliance with
the E&S risk management measures and the PIU will submit it to the World Bank. Key indicators to be used
for evaluation will measure sub-project sustainability and public participation and ownership.


5.2 Implementation Arrangements:
The Project will be implemented by the MILLS through a National PIU. The PIU will coordinate the regional
PIUs for the implementation of Component 2, and it will coordinate with MoWE on the implementation
of Component 3, and with the MoA on the implementation of Component 4. The MILLS PIU which will
include a functional environmental and social risk management organizational structure. It will consist of
one Environmental, one Social Specialist and one OHS Specialist. They will be full time project staff. The
Environmental and the Social Specialists will be responsible for the monitoring of the compliance with this
ESMF including the LMP, SEA/SH Action Plan, and the SEP. Where necessary, they will discuss corrective
measures with the PSC. They will further oversee the preparation of site-specific additional E&S
instruments, such as ESIAs, ESMPs, or RAPs for sub-projects. The will receive these instruments for review
and for onward submission to the World Bank.

At the federal level, MILLs has an EHS unit which supports Project preparation and will have a dedicated
PIU which will be staffed by Environmental, Social and Occupational Health and Safety Specialists. The E&S
Specialists at the PIU will be responsible for the monitoring and supervision of the subproject-specific E&S
instruments for all Project Components. The Social Specialists, in addition, will ensure the implementation
of all stakeholder engagement requirements, and the implementation of the GRM, the LMP and the
SEA/SH Action Plan. The E&S Specialists will further oversee any correctional measures, and will be
responsible for regular reporting on the E&S implementation to the World Bank as part of the quarterly
Project Progress Reports.

The regional governments will be a key implementer of Component 2, under the oversight of the MILLS
PIU. For this purpose, the regional governments will use their existing structures, including their existing
Environmental and Social Specialists. Where such do not exist, they will be recruited for the purpose of


                                                                                                             29
                                                                                                            Official Use Only
this Project. The E&S Specialists at the regional governments will be responsible for the monitoring and
supervision of the subproject-specific E&S instruments in regard to Component 2 activities. They will assist
in the preparation of bidding documents for the rehabilitation activities and ensure that all subproject-
specific requirements are included in the bidding documents and that the construction companies have
the respective capacity to implement the requirements. They will undertake field monitoring missions and
review documentation in order to monitor the implementation of the E&S requirements, and they will
train regional level government staff in monitoring efforts.

For Component 3, the MILLS PIU directly and the MoWE will act as key proposers of sub-projects. For
activities under Component 3, the PIU E&S Specialists will be directly responsible for the preparation and
implementation of sub-project E&S instruments and for reporting on such. Where the MoWE acts as a
proposer of a sub-project, the WoWE will be responsible for the screening, preparation of E&S instruments
and reporting on the compliance with these instruments. For this purpose the MoWE will ensure the
availability of one Environmental and one Social Specialist.

The PIU E&S Specialists will receive quarterly ESHS performance reports from all proposers/implementers.
The PIU will submit these reports, together with a Project Progress Report to the World Bank on a quarterly
basis. The PIU E&S staff will flag any performance concerns or non-compliance with the PIU leadership.

Contractors: Contractors will be implementing E&S mitigation measures as laid out in the ESMF and
subsequent ESMPs under Component 2 and 3 construction and rehabilitation activities. Mitigation
measures requirement will be included in all procurement and bidding documentation, including in Bills
of Quantities (BoQs), and will be cost in agreements with the contractors. The contractors will be obliged
to ensure that staff with EHS experience and capacity is involved in construction works and can fulfill the
reporting requirements on E&S, and can guide and supervise all workers, including community workers.

Dam Safety Panel of Experts: For Component 3 activities, the PIU will recruit and maintain a Dam Safety
Panel of Experts, with terms of reference acceptable to the World Bank, to oversee dam safety for all dams
associated with targeted irrigation schemes under the Activity.

The dam safety panel of experts will a) review and advise on dam safety aspects relevant to the feasibility
and design studies; b) ensure that dam safety considerations are integrated into all preparatory activities,
in accordance with Good International Industry Practice (GIIP) and World Bank requirements; c) provide
written recommendations for dam safety measures to be considered in subsequent project phases, should
the project proceed to construction; d) integrate dam safety risk assessment and recommendations into
feasibility/design studies and ESIAs.
