Annex 8: Integrated Pest Management Framework (IPMF)
Objectives
The Integrated Pest Management Framework (IPMF) is aligned with the World Bank’s ESS3 on Resource
Efficiency and Pollution Prevention and Management. The main objective of the IPMP is to ensure that
pest management is environmentally sounds, socially acceptable and economically viable, while
minimizing risks to human health and the environment.

The purpose of this section is to provide a framework for the integration of pest management
considerations in the planning and implementation of the activities to be implemented within the project
specifically on activities that will involve use of fertilizers and pesticides for agricultural production or that
result in agricultural activities during the operational phase. Use of pesticides can constitute increased
risks to human health and environment. The IPMF promotes the safe, effective and sustainable
management of pests and pesticides to a) reduce reliance on chemical pesticides; b) encourage biological
and ecological pest control methods, and c) prevent adverse impacts on human health, biodiversity, soil,
and water quality associated with pesticide use in irrigation-supported agriculture.

Environmental and Social risks and impacts of Pesticides
The potential adverse effects of pesticide use within the related activities under the project will include
among others:

    •   Pollution and contamination of surface and underground water bodies through pesticide runoff
        or leaching into surface and groundwater
    •   Contamination of rivers, irrigation canals and wetlands =, harming the aquatic ecosystem.
    •   Accumulation of pesticide residues in soils, reducing soil fertility and microbial diversity
    •   Impact on terrestrial fauna due to contamination, such as beneficial insects. And non-target
        species (fish, birds, and amphibians)
    •   Human and animal health related hazards due to indiscriminate exposure to pesticides.
    •   Overuse or misuse of pesticides may lead to pest populations developing resistance, resulting in
        greater chemical dependence.
    •   Human health and safety risks, including through farmer and workers exposure, community
        exposure and improper handling and disposal

All the pesticides banned or under restriction in Ethiopia will not be used in the project. Wherever possible,
non-chemical means of pest control will be exercised, in keeping with the promotion of promote
Integrated Pest Management (IPM). IPM can in some cases facilitate agricultural pest protection without
the application of chemical-based pesticides. Some IPM tactics that could be considered by project
activities that reduce pest risk include:

    •   Biological control methods
    •   Natural physical methods, such as picking of ticks
    •   Mechanical/manual control of pests
    •   Growing of pest resistant crops.



                                                                                                             126


                                                                                                              Official Use Only
IPM Planning, Design & Implementation
This sub-section describes the typical methods that should be used by the project when preparing IPM for
activities specifically when environmental and social screening determines that pesticides will be utilized
and or their use will be promoted in the specific project activity.

IPM is a sustainable approach to managing pests by combining cultural, physical/mechanical, biological,
and chemical tools in a way that keep pests below their economic injury levels and minimizes economic,
health and environmental risks. Fundamentally, IPM aims to maximize the use of biological control; other
control measures, especially chemicals, play a supportive rather than a disruptive role. Every activity under
the project that includes pest management will require the preparation of a site-specific IPM Plan (site-
specific IPMP) and the sections below outline the proposed approach in developing site- specific IPMPs.
The project will utilize its E&S specialists and trained professionals with expertise in agricultural science
and pest management to prepare these site- specific IPMPs.

The following are preconditions for an IPM approach, which will be adopted by all project activities that
will be screened and found to require pesticide use:

    a) Understanding of the ecological relationships within a farming system (crops, plants, pests,
       organisms) and factors influencing their development
    b) Understanding of economic factors within a production system (infestation, loss)
    c) Understanding of socio-cultural decision-making behavior of the farmers (traditional preferences,
       risk behavior)
    d) Involvement of the farmers in the analysis of the pest problems and their management
    e) Successive creation of a legislative and agricultural policy framework conducive to a sustainable
       IPM strategy (plant quarantine legislation, pesticides legislation, pesticide registration, price
       policy).

This Project IPMP has been developed following and applying the key elements of IPM, namely:

    1. Ensuring that activities/subprojects under the project use available, suitable, and compatible
       methods which include resistant varieties, cultural methods, biological control, safe pesticides,
       etc. to maintain pests below levels that cause economic damage and loss
    2. Ensuring that activities/subprojects under project conserve the ecosystem to enhance and
       support natural pollinators
    3. Ensuring that activities/subprojects under project integrate the pest management strategies in the
       farming systems
    4. Ensuring that activities/subprojects under project conduct analysis based on pests and crop loss
       assessments.

Setting up IPM
Planning is at the core of any IPM. Every irrigation farm has pests that need to be considered. Waiting until
problems arise will increase reliance on pesticides more and more. Good IPM has four parts and all the
site-specific IPMPs that will be prepared under the project will have to follow these parts:

    a)   Identifying problems
    b)   Selecting tactics
    c)   Considering economic and environmental factors
    d)   Evaluating the program.

                                                                                                         127


                                                                                                         Official Use Only
A. Proper Identification of Problems
The correct IPM approach promotes “proactive” rather than “reactive” management. Correct
identification is the first and most important step in controlling a problem. This first step is critical to future
success, since an incorrect diagnosis leads to mismanagement. Scouting is, in fact, the key feature of any
IPM. By scouting, the ability to detect potential problems early is increased. The earlier a problem is
discovered, the better your chances are of avoiding economic losses. Primary beneficiaries of the project
will use pesticides or promote use pesticides and will be trained in scouting techniques to identify pests
early to promote the proactive approach in pest management as described above. To scout effectively, the
above-mentioned primary beneficiaries of the project will be encouraged and trained to:

    •   Identify the cause of the problem to find out what kind of pests they are dealing with.
    •   Contact agricultural extension officers or DAs if they encounter something that they cannot
        identify.
    •   Determine the stage of growth of the pests and crops. This is essential for proper timing of control
        methods.
    •   Decide whether the infestation is increasing or decreasing.
    •   Assess the condition of the crops.
    •   Map out problem areas. It may be possible to limit the area that needs treatment.
    •   Use the right scouting method for the specific pest.

B. Select Tactics
Once the problem has been identified, considerations on how to control it will have to be made. The goal
in selecting control tactics is to use methods that are effective, practical, economical, and
environmentally sound. To select the best control tactics, there is a need to:

    •   Understand the life cycle and habits of the pests. Some control methods will work only if they are
        used at the right time.
    •   Decide whether the infestation is serious in terms of economic loss.
    •   Compare the costs and benefits of various control methods.
    •   Make plans for the future. Not every part of IPM can be put into effect immediately.

Sampling to determine the extent of the problem: Once the pest is correctly identified, the next question
which will need to be answered will be, Is there a risk of significant loss? Is the problem occasionally seen?
Localized? Or commonly found throughout? What is the extent of the damage? Is the problem a growing
threat? Correct sampling will help to eliminate the guesswork in pest control by providing a means to
quantify an old problem or discover a new one. Sampling knowledge and information on pests and crop
biology should be used to make better management decisions.

Analysis to assess problem importance: This step in the pest management process entails analyzing the
identification and sampling information and evaluating the need for a pest control action to make a
determination on how bad the problem really is. There is need to assess whether the potential control
measures are more costly than the damage potential? There is need to weigh economic, environmental,
and times concerns and assess the impact of the current pest control decision on future management
decisions.



                                                                                                              128


                                                                                                               Official Use Only
Selection of appropriate management alternative: The action plan needed must entail a strategy that fits
with the short- and long-term plans, labor force, capital, equipment, and finances of the farm. Therefore,
an evaluation of the costs, benefits, and risks of employing various management options is needed.
Opportunities to integrate different pest control strategies must be considered.

C. Choosing Controls
There is no such thing as a completely safe and natural pesticide. Pesticides can vary greatly in their level
of toxicity to non-target organisms such as people, pets or beneficial insects. Even organically approved
pesticides can pose a danger to people and the environment if they are not used properly.

Pesticides are grouped into various types or categories. For example, there are fungicides, insecticides,
herbicides (for weeds), nematicides (for nematodes) and miticides (for mites).

While many pesticides control specific problems, there are still some broad-spectrum controls. In addition,
there are many products that are only effective if they are used at a specific growth stage of the pest, so
timing is critical.

It is never wise to use blanket applications of pesticides on large areas or to use them based on a calendar
alone. The improper use of pesticides can pose a risk to the applicator, family, plants, beneficial organisms
and the environment. There are times when pesticides are needed to prevent major losses. The judicious
and proper use of pesticides can occur with a sound IPM program. The following are examples of
management tactics which can be employed:

    •   Biological - Parasites, predators, pest
    •   Chemical - Pesticides, pheromones, baits, attractants
    •   Physical

Consider Economic Factors: Know When It Pays to Use a Pesticide Despite efforts to avoid using chemicals,
there are times when only pesticides can control the damage. Even so, it may not pay to use them.
Pesticides should be used in an IPM program only when the benefits (yield, quality, aesthetic value) exceed
the costs of control. Otherwise, time and money are wasted. It’s not easy to figure out when it pays to use
pesticides. There are many variables: the pest population, variety, and growth stage, weather, and cost of
the control.

D. Evaluate IPM
Evaluation means deciding how effective a program is and whether any changes are needed. All
subprojects that will prepare site-specific IPMPs will have to evaluate IPM, and should:

    •    Monitor fields and keep records: Each time field visits are made, a note of crops and pest
        conditions, record yields and quality and record any counts on pest populations.
    •   Record control measures: Records should include dates, weather conditions, pest levels,
        application rates and timing, and costs. Good records are a guide if the same problem occurs.
    •   Compare effectiveness: Whatever control tactics are chosen, use a different method on some
        strips. That way comparison can be made, which worked better, taking into account costs and
        environmental impacts.




                                                                                                         129


                                                                                                         Official Use Only
Reporting and Performance Review Requirements: The MILLs through the PIU will integrate pesticide use
activities within the regular project specific quarterly progress safeguards reports for all subprojects,
including site-specific IPMPs for submission to the World Bank specifically for identified sub-projects
during the feasibility study phase of the sub-projects following detailed screening.

Pest Management for Agriculture Subprojects:
Project activities may involve strengthening existing practices, introducing, diversifying or the
intensification of crop production. Support for the development of large-, medium, and small-scale
irrigation may lead to the introduction or increased use of pesticides and other agricultural chemicals such
as herbicides and fertilizers. Pests are organisms that compete with humans, domestic animals, or crops
for nutritional resources. They include species of insects, mites, nematodes, mollusks, plant pathogens,
vertebrates and weeds. Fertilizers are used to promote crop growth. It is critical that appropriate planning,
design and management be adopted for the handling, use, and management of all agricultural chemicals
to avoid potential negative environmental impacts. If appropriate for the irrigation sub-projects, the site-
specific pest management plan should address the following issues:

    1. Proper use of agricultural chemicals such as fertilizers to avoid reduction in soil and groundwater
       quality.
    2. Prevent fertilizer run-off into surface water sources to avoid negative impacts on aquatic
       environments.
    3. Proper use of pesticides and herbicides to avoid contamination of crops, soils and water.
    4. Proper use, handling and storage of all agricultural chemicals to avoid adverse health impacts on
       the rural population.
    5. Ensure that banned or unauthorized agricultural chemicals are not used.
    6. Proper handling and disposal of unused agricultural chemicals and packaging materials (i.e. sacks,
       plastic containers, etc.).

Guideline for site-specific Integrated Pest Management Plan for Irrigation Sub-projects

Pest Management Approach

     i. Current and anticipated pest problems relevant to the project: common pest problems and
        estimated economic impact
    ii. Current and proposed pest management practices: Describe current and proposed practices,
        including non-chemical preventative techniques, biological and chemical control. Is optimum use
        being made of agro-ecosystem management techniques to reduce pest pressure and of available
        non-chemical methods to control pests? Do farmers and extension staffs get sufficient information
        about IPM approaches that reduce reliance on chemical control?
   iii. Relevant IPM experience within the project area, woreda, region or country describe existing IPM
        practices, projects/programs, research
   iv. Assessment of proposed or current pest management approach and recommendations for
        adjustment, where necessary. Where the current or proposed practices are not consistent with
        the principles of an IPM approach, the discrepancies should be discussed, and a strategy should
        be proposed to bring pest management activities into line with IPM.

Pesticide Management
  i.    Describe present, proposed and/or envisaged pesticide use and assess whether such use is in line
        with IPM principles. Provide purpose of pesticide use, type of products used, frequency of

                                                                                                         130


                                                                                                         Official Use Only
        applications, and application methods. Does pesticide use part of an IPM approach and is it
        justified? Justification of pesticide use under the project should (a) explain the IPM approach and
        the reason why pesticide use is considered, (b) provide an economic assessment demonstrating
        that the proposed pesticide use would increase farmers’ net profits, or for public health projects,
        provide evidence that the proposed pesticide use is justified from the best available (probably
        WHO supported evidence) public health evidence.
  ii.   Indication of type and quantity of pesticides envisaged to be financed by the project (in volume
        and ETB value) and/or assessment of increase in pesticide use resulting from the project.
 iii.   Circumstances of pesticide use and the capability and competence of end-users to handle
        products within acceptable risk margins (e.g. user access to, and use of, protective gears and
        appropriate application equipment; user’s product knowledge and understanding of hazards and
        risks; appropriateness of on-farm storage facilities for pesticide).
 iv.    Assessment of environmental, occupational and public health risks associated with the transport,
        storage, handling and use of the proposed products under local circumstances, and the disposal
        of empty containers.
  v.    Pre-requisites and/or measures required to reduce specific risks associated with envisaged
        pesticide use under the project (e.g.: protective gear, training, upgrading of storage facilities, etc.).
 vi.    Selection of pesticides authorized for use, taking into consideration: (a) criteria set at national (if
        there is any) or international, (b) the hazards and risks and (c) the availability of newer or less
        hazardous products and techniques (e.g. bio-pesticides, traps).

Monitoring and Supervision
   i.  Description of activities that require local monitoring during implementation.
  ii.  Description of activities that require monitoring during supervision visits (e.g. regarding
       effectiveness of measures to mitigate risks; progress in strengthening regulatory framework and
       institutional capacity; identification of new issues or risks arising during implementation).
 iii.  Monitoring and supervision plan, implementation responsibilities, required expertise and budget.

two, the more rigorous measures apply.

Annex 11: Voluntary Land Donation Procedures
Voluntary Land Donation (VLD) procedures applies when a household wishes to make a voluntary
donation of land for the purpose of subproject implementation. In the context of Ethiopia, where land is
owned by the Government, “land donation” is taken to mean “donation of land use”. In some
circumstances, it may be proposed that part or all of the land to be used by a sub-project is donated on a
voluntary basis without payment of full compensation. This may be acceptable providing the Project
demonstrates that:
    • The potential donor or donors including their family members have been appropriately informed
        and consulted about the project and the choices available to them;
    • Potential donors are aware that refusal is an option, and have confirmed in writing their
        willingness to proceed with the donation;
    • It should be verified that the donation does not result from any form of coercion or manipulation
        and is offered in good faith;
    • The amount of land being donated is minor and will not reduce the donor’s remaining land area
        below that required to maintain the donor’s livelihood;
    • The proportion of land that may be donated should not significantly affect the donor’s livelihood;
        voluntary land donation cannot exceed 10% of an individual’s total land holdings;
    • Voluntary land donation should not be resulted to household relocation or physical displacement,
        loss of structures or fixed assets on affected portion of land;
    • The donor is expected to benefit directly from the project;
    • Community or collective land donation can only occur with the agreement of individuals using or
        occupying the land i.e. verification of the voluntary nature of land donations must be obtained
        from each person donating the use of land; the Project will maintain a transparent record of all
        consultations and agreements reached;
    • If community services are to be provided under the project, land title must be vested in the
        community;
    • The land to be donated must be free of other claims or encumbrances; and
    • Grievance Redress Mechanisms (GRM) must be available

As an indicative procedure to consider in voluntary donation of land, the following points provide guidance
on the process:
          • Verify the requirements of the donation and the formalization of the donation;
          • Carry out due diligence on the owners and users of land donated;
          • Ensure appropriate consultation and disclosure;
          • Establish informed consent of the person donating the land;
          • Sign written agreement; and
          • Establish grievance redress mechanism.
Annex 12: TOR for ESIAs under Component 1 Activities
                                                            Terms of Reference

 ENVIRONMENTAL & SOCIAL IMPACT ASSESSMENT (ESIA) FOR GODE, GIDABO, ADA’A BECHO, KELAFO,
                    ASGORI AND SEBETA HAWAS IRRIGATION SCHEMES

BACKGROUND

Sector Context

While the agricultural sector in Ethiopia is the backbone of the country’s economy and jobs, the
productivity is low due to heavy reliance on rain-fed agriculture. The agricultural sector accounts for 33
percent of GDP, 73 percent of employment, and 77 percent of exports.17 Despite its significant role,
agricultural productivity remains very low, primarily due to the heavy reliance on traditional rain-fed
farming. Erratic and increasingly unpredictable rainfall leads to inconsistent and often insufficient crop
yields. The Government of Ethiopia (GoE) has set ambitious goals to reduce reliance on rain-fed agriculture
by developing irrigation capacity. The Ten-Year Development Plan of Ethiopia (2020-2030)18 aims to expand
irrigation, promote private sector participation, increase crop production fivefold through irrigation, and
create over 1 million job opportunities in irrigation sector.

Rehabilitation and modernization of existing irrigation schemes offer opportunities to boost irrigation
capacity in Ethiopia. Most existing irrigation schemes in Ethiopia exhibit low performance, due to poor
design and construction, a lack of quality assurance system, limited institutional and community capacity,
inadequate funding, and insufficient operation and maintenance (O&M). The existing schemes have
deteriorated significantly, failing to fulfill irrigation demands. The performance of existing schemes is
generally below 50 percent in terms of irrigated areas, crop intensity and yields. Rehabilitating and
modernizing the existing schemes can boost irrigation capacity within a relatively short period at a lower
cost, improve resilience to droughts and floods, and enhance food security.

A new ministry, the Ministry of Irrigation and Lowlands (MILLs), was established in 2021 to lead irrigation
development in Ethiopia. Previously, the Ministry of Water, Irrigation and Energy (MoWIE) oversaw
irrigation development, while the Ministry of Agriculture (MoA) managed small-scale irrigation
development. MILLs is mandated to develop and implement irrigation policies, strategies, and laws; design
and implement irrigation projects; and promote private sector participation. MILLs is responsible for
constructing, operating and managing federal-level large- and medium-scale irrigation schemes, as well as
providing technical support and capacity building to the regions for regional-level medium- and small-scale
irrigation project.

Climate Resilient Irrigation for Sustainable Productivity Component 1.

The proposed World Bank-financed Climate Resilient Irrigation for Sustainable Production (CRIPS) Project
plans to strengthen the capacity of the irrigation sector, as well as support the development of National
Irrigation Information System (NIIS) and Quality Assurance System. Development Objective (PDO) of the
Project is to improve access to resilient irrigation services for sustainable production in Ethiopia.

17 Ministry of Agriculture, Ethiopia’s National Agricultural Investment Plan (2021-2030) (link)
18
     Ten-Year Development Plan of Ethiopia (2020-2030) (link)
The CRISP consists of five components:

Component 1: Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization
Component 2: Small-Scale Irrigation Development
Component 3: Dam Safety and irrigation Storage Improvement
Component 4: Irrigation Service Improvement
Component 5: Project Mangement and Capacity Building

This TOR is in reference to activities planned under Component 1.

Component 1: Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization. Component 1 will support the rehabilitation
and modernization of existing federal-level large- and medium-scale irrigation schemes. Comprehensive
screening and prioritization criteria were co-developed. Six irrigation schemes—Gode, Gidabo, Ada’a
Becho, Kelafo, Asgori and Sebeta Hawas —have been prioritized, each at a different stage of readiness. At
least two of these schemes have recent feasibility studies and detailed designs, which will be advance. In
addition, Component 1 will support the development of the Investment Plan by financing the necessary
studies and designs for pipeline projects. Component 1 will be primarily led and implemented by MILLS.
Key activities include:

Irrigation Rehabilitation and Extension: CRISP will support the rehabilitation of key irrigation
infrastructure. In several schemes, while dams/boreholes and main canals have already been completed,
the absence of secondary and tertiary canals continues to hinder water delivery to the fields. To address
this, CRISP will also finance critical extension works to complete the distribution network. All
interventions will include improvements of on-farm systems to ensure that irrigation water effectively
reaches the farm level and contributes to agricultural productivity.

Irrigation Modernization: CRISP will support the modernization of existing schemes to enhance water
use efficiency, irrigation energy optimization, and agricultural productivity. Modernization efforts will be
tailored to the local context, with careful consideration of cost-effectiveness, community capacity for
O&M, and long-term sustainability.

Investment Plan Preparation: Preparing pipeline projects for rolling out the rehabilitation and
modernization investments in the Investment Plan, including preparing feasibility studies, detailed
designs (FS/DD), site-specific E&S instruments, and bidding documents.

Design already available will be shared with the consultant.

Purpose of the TOR
Under Component 1 (Investment Plan Preparation), the Project intends to contract a qualified consulting
firm to prepare site-specific Environmental and Social Impact Assessments (ESIAs) for six priority irrigation
schemes—Gode, Gidabo, Ada’a Becho, Kelafo, Asgori, and Sebeta Hawas—to inform the design and
implementation of planned rehabilitation and modernization works.

The main purpose of this TOR is to provide an overview of the methodology to be used as well as key
activities expected in the preparation of the ESIA. This TOR provides general guidance for the following
major activities. Conduct an ESIA, which is intended to address all environmental and social issues that are
likely to arise during the planning and design, construction/rehabilitation, operation, and
decommissioning phases while taking into account national laws and regulations as well as the World Bank
Environment and Social Standards (ESS) and Guidelines. A gap analysis between Ethiopian laws and the
applicable World Bank ESS is expected in order to identify legal gaps and close them.

The consultant is in charge of preparing the ESIA report addressing the comments/feedback provided by
the Ministry and the World Bank and prepare the relevant documents for approval by the World Bank
(WB) and the Ethiopia Environmental Protection Authority (EPA).

OBJECTIVES OF THE CONSULTANCY SERVICE
The consultant is required to prepare a total of six ESIA for Gode, Gidabo, Ada’a Becho, Kelafo, Asgori and
Sebeta Hawas irrigation systems . The main objectives of the consultancy is to:

Conduct participatory consultation and engagements with different level of stakeholders and project
affected communities for the impact assessment and the identification of E&S mitigation measures.
Identify baseline data on the physical and biological environment, as well as the social, gender equality
issues, cultural heritage aspects, demographic, and economic characteristics of the population in and
around the selected sites.

Assess all environmental and social issues that are likely to arise during the planning,
construction/rehabilitation, operation, and decommissioning phases of the irrigation systems while taking
into account national laws and regulations as well as the World relevant World Bank ESS.
Prepare a gap analysis between Ethiopian laws and the relevant World Bank ESS and make
recommendations how to close gaps.

Identify potential environmental and socio-economic receptors and impacts associated with the planned
sub-project activities and suggest mitigation and monitoring measures so adverse impacts do not
outweigh the expected positive environmental benefits.

Discuss potential alternative designs or activities in order to avoid identified E&S risks and impacts.

SUB-PROJECT TO BE STUDIED UNDER THE ESIA:
I. Gode Irrigation Rehabilitation and Modernization

SCOPE OF WORK AND EXPECTED OUTPUT
The scope of the required consultancy service is to conduct an ESIA in order to assess the existing
environmental and social context, identify project impacts/ risks and propose mitigation measures and
management options. The study is expected to ensure the project complies with the country’s legal
guidelines and the World Bank environmental and social standards policies and requirements.
Additionally, the study’s outcomes will serve as a critical foundation for informed decision-making, guiding
the project towards practices that safeguard environmental and social well-being, and promote long-term
sustainability for the surrounding communities and ecosystems.

GENERAL
In general, the Consultant will perform the following key tasks as part of the five ESIAs.:
    • Describe the Subprojects including its main components, ancillary components/facilities,
        resources input, and outputs/produces.
    • Establish baseline information on the environmental and relevant social aspects of the respective
        area of influence.
    •   List legal and institutional frameworks under which the Subprojects will be implemented and
        operated.
    •   Identify beneficial and adverse environmental and social risks and impacts of the Subprojects
        during planning, construction/rehabilitation, operation and decommissioning in alignment with
        the Bank Environmental and Social Standards.
    •   Propose suitable, practical, and site-specific enhancement and mitigation measures to avoid,
        reduce, mitigate, and compensate/offset the identified risks and impacts with operational details.
    •   Conduct meaningful stakeholder consultations to capture the Subproject-affected people and
        parties’ aspirations, concerns, and recommendations towards the planned interventions.
    •   Carry out and analysis of alternatives and compare various Subproject planning and design options
        in terms of their environmental and social consequences.
    •   Develop environmental and social management and monitoring plans with institutional
        arrangement, human resources, and budget requirement.
    •   Develop specific environmental and social requirements and procedures to be included in
        contractor’s contract documents.

Further, the details of the various activities and outputs of the ESIA are described in the below sections.

INTRODUCTION
    • At the onset of the ESIAs, the following the description of the respective Subproject should be
       provided.
    • The Project (the CRISP) background/context
    • The specific Subproject background/context
    • Rationale for the Subproject
    • The objectives of the ESIA
    • The scope of the ESIA
    • Approach/methodology of the ESIA
    • The structure of the ESIA report.

LEGAL AND INSTITUTIONAL FRAMEWORK
    • A detailed review of relevant national, regional, international and World Bank regulatory
        frameworks shall be made to establish the setting against which the Subproject will be
        implemented. The review shall include (but not limited to):
    • Applicable national policies and strategies for water and irrigation:
    • Applicable national legislations (proclamation, regulations, directives, guidelines) for water and
        irrigation, environmental and social management
    • Applicable relevant regional and international conventions and protocols (ratified by Ethiopia)
    • Applicable regional agreements, cooperative frameworks, guidelines, etc. including on energy
        sector and associated environmental and social management
    • The World Bank’s Environmental and Social Framework (ESF), the relevant Environmental and
        Social Standards (ESSs), specifically:
    • ESS1 Assessment and Management of Environmental and Social Risks and Impacts
    • ESS2 Labor and Working Conditions
    • ESS3 Resource Efficiency and Pollution Prevention and Management
    • ESS4 Community Health and Safety
    • ESS5 Land Acquisition, Restrictions on Land Use and Involuntary Resettlement
    • ESS6 Biodiversity Conservation and Sustainable Management of Living Natural Resources
    •   ESS7 Indigenous Peoples/Sub-Saharan African Historically Underserved Traditional Local
        Communities
    •   ESS8 Cultural Heritage
    •   ESS10 Stakeholder Engagement and Information Disclosure
    •   Operational Policy 7.50 on Projects on International Waterways
    •   Applicable guidelines, such as the WBG Environmental, Health, and Safety (EHS) Guidelines

In addition, the national, regional, and local institutional frameworks under which the Subproject will be
implemented shall be identified and assessed as part of the ESIA. Relevant institutions, their mandates,
institutional structure, and capacities shall be identified. The key institutions including Regional and
woreda land administration offices, city administrations, and independent asset valuators.

Other relevant organizations and institutions shall be identified, and their structures, relationships, roles,
responsibilities, and capacities shall be assessed and described.

SUBPROJECT DESCRIPTION
The Subprojects shall be described comprehensively including the following, but not limited to:
    •   The CRISP Project
    •   The Project development objective (PDO)
    •   The Project components and subcomponents
    •   The Project environmental and social risks ratings
    •   The Project beneficiaries
    •   The Project implementation arrangements
    •   The specific Subproject
    •   The location of the Subproject (geographic and administrative locations), with location map(s)
    •   Specific site location
    •   Accessibility of the site
    •   The Subproject design and its main components
    •   Construction materials used for construction of various components of the Subproject including sources of
        the construction materials (market or own source)
    •   Machineries to be used during construction and operation of the Subproject
    •   Energy consumption by the Subproject
    •   Waste generated from the Subproject
    •   Human resources requirements during implementation and operation phases in detail
    •   Subproject       implementation      activities   (during   planning,   construction/rehabiliation,   and
        operation/maintenance stages)
    •   Respective land and resettlement requirements
    •   List of activities for the implementation of the Subproject
    •   Subproject implementation schedule.
    •   The Subproject description shall form a basis for the ESIA including to establish environmental and social
        baseline conditions, identifying and prioritizing key environmental and social issues, and recommending
        measures to avoid, reduce, mitigate, and offset the identified impacts.

ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL BASELINE CONDITIONS
Environmental and social baseline conditions against which the Subproject will be implemented shall be
described in detail.

Define the Area of influence (AoI):
It is essential that the delineation of the Area of influence (AoI) be justified in the ESIA, drawing on
stakeholder input, baseline studies, and expert judgment to ensure all significant impacts are captured
and addressed.

Thus, the ESIA study shall determine the area of influence based on hydrological boundaries,
administrative limits, and the connectivity of social and ecological systems and consideration of:
    •   The entire physical footprint of the irrigation infrastructure (canals, pumping stations, reservoirs, etc.)
    •   The command area receives water from the rehabilitated system
    •   Downstream areas affected by changes in water flow, quality, or sedimentation
    •   Adjacent and potentially impacted ecological and social features (e.g., wetlands, villages, protected areas)

identify the basic environmental and social baseline conditions of the Sub-project area of influence shall
include: the ESIA must extend beyond the immediate scope of proposed works and thoughtfully integrate
considerations related to the existing irrigation scheme which is a base for the current rehabilitation work.
This approach acknowledges that the environmental and social context, as well as potential impacts, are
shaped not only by forthcoming interventions but also by the characteristics and legacy of the operational
system already in place. This includes a precise delineation of the rehabilitation project, specifying
objectives, locations, project scale, and nature of the works to be undertaken. In addition, there must be
a thorough inventory and description of the existing irrigation infrastructure, including its age, design
capacity, layout, operational status, historical modifications, and current management practices. This is
essential to contextualize the rehabilitation within the broader system and to identify pre-existing
conditions and constraints.
    •   The physical environment (climate, topography, geology/geomorphology, soils, water resources, irrigation
        modalities, land use, land cover, etc.)
    •   The biological environment (flora, fauna, key biodiversity areas, natural, critical, and modified habitats)
    •   The socio-economic environment (demography, ethnic composition, traditionally underserved
        communities, social structure, religion, cultural heritage sites, settlement patterns/land uses, livelihood
        strategies, household income and employment, food security, access to social services and utilities,
        community level irrigation arrangements, water committees etc.).
    •   Land Ownership and Use: Current arrangements, tenure, potential for displacement or resettlement
    •   The environmental and social baseline conditions shall be done through desk study and field surveys. Gaps
        and reliability of baseline data used for the description shall be identified and indicated in the assessment.

ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL RISKS & IMPACTS
The ESIAs shall identify and evaluate the significance of potential environmental and social risks and
impacts that will result from implementation of the Subprojects. The ESIAs shall focus on environmental
and social effects that are significant in their likelihood and consequences. Further, the assessment shall
consider both, beneficial/positive and adverse/negative impacts. The nature of the impacts shall be
described as direct/indirect/induced/cumulative, temporary/long-term, local/regional, and
reversible/irreversible and the impact of the Project activity shall be indicated separately. The Sub-
projects’ adverse Risk/impacts and mitigation measures shall be identified including (but not limited to)
the following:

ESS1: Assessment and Management of Environmental and Social Risks and Impacts: Define the project
area of influence, including associated facilities, and identify potential cumulative impacts to be assessed
proportionately. Analyze reasonable alternatives (location, technology/design, construction
methods/schedules, and “no project” alternative) with justification for the preferred option and
embedded avoidance/minimization measures.
Sub project level Institutional E&S capacity assessment and requirement, ESCP requirements, impact on
vulnerable and disadvantaged people; Potential alteration of water regimes, affecting downstream users
and ecosystems; Adverse impacts on water quality due to agrochemical runoff and increased
sedimentation; Increased flooding or waterlogging if infrastructure is improperly designed.

ESS2: Labor and Working condition: Identify all categories of project workers: direct workers
(PIU/consultants), contracted workers (contractors/subcontractors), primary supply workers, and any
community workers if applicable. Characterize labor influx risks, workforce size, skill mix, seasonality, and
any need for worker accommodation. Prepare a concise Labor Risk Profile to inform instrument design
and supervision focus.

Occupational Health and Safety (OHS): Physical hazards including; rotating and moving
equipment/machinery, noise and vibration, electrical hazard, eye hazards due to flying debris, hazards
from hot works, traffic accidents from the site traffic and offsite movements of Sub-project vehicles/trucks,
excavation hazards, ergonomics, repetitive motion, manual handling, working environment temperature
and illumination, poor housekeeping, slip, trip, fall; chemical hazards including; poor air quality, fire and
explosions, hazardous chemical; biological hazards including; exposure to wastewater, transmittable
diseases/infections; potential use of child labor and/or forced labor, and special hazard environments such
as confined space, lone and isolated workers; non-compliance with labor conditions; risks of Sexual
Exploitation and Abuse (SEA) and Sexual Harassment (SH) among workers, etc.

ESS3-Resource Efficiency and Pollution Prevention and Management: Air emissions/fugitive dust and
ambient air quality impacts; impact of loss of soil and land degradation; impact on water quality; noise
and vibration impacts; impacts of waste generation and disposal; Release of hazardous materials such as
fuel or lubricants from machinery and impact of hazardous waste; impact due to non-hazardous waste,
visual impacts; Overuse or inefficient use of water resources, leading to depletion of aquifers or surface
water; Increased chemical use (fertilizers, pesticides) resulting in soil and water contamination. Identify
primary suppliers sources for materials, including quarries/borrow pits, sand mining areas, clay borrow
areas etc.

ESS4-Community Health and Safety: Map receptors and sensitive locations, settlements, schools, clinics,
markets, farm access routes, and community use of canal embankments/inspection roads. Identify CHS
risk pathways; traffic and haulage, trenching/open pits, increased heavy machinery presence, labor influx
and camps, SEA/SH risk, materials storage, hazardous waste handling, noise/dust near communities, and
any reservoirs/dams linked to the scheme requiring dam safety due diligence under ESS4. Describe legacy
infrastructure risks, e.g., existing storages, dams or diversion structures that may influence downstream
safety; plan the necessary dam safety assessments per ESS4 where relevant. Propose site-specific traffic
management, emergency preparedness, and communicable disease and SEA/SH risk management plans.
Assess the security context and risks associated with engaging security personnel to safeguard project sites
and assets, including potential risks to community members and workers. Develop a proportionate
Security Management Plan consistent with ESS4 and applicable national laws, aligned with good
international industry practice.

ESS5: Land Acquisition, Restrictions on Land Use, and Involuntary Resettlement: Define the project area
of influence and map all components likely to cause land impacts. , Identify potential land needs,
temporary easements, access restrictions, and asset/livelihood impacts (including crops, trees, irrigation
structures, and farm access).Where land was acquired in the last five years or earlier for facilities that will
be integrated into the project, or where there are legacy land/resettlement issues conduct ex-post social
audits to assess consistency with ESS5.
Prepare a site-specific mitigation plan that is proportionate to the impact and covers the project footprint,
identification of risks and impacts, proposed mitigation and monitoring measures, roles and
responsibilities, timelines, budget, and stakeholder engagement and grievance redress procedures.

 ESS6: Biodiversity Conservation and Sustainable Management of Living Natural Resources:
impact due to clearing of vegetation cover and disturbance of habitat; biodiversity loss; impacts on
protected habitats, loss or fragmentation of natural habitats due to expanded irrigation infrastructure;
impacts on aquatic and terrestrial ecosystems from altered hydrology and water quality etc. The ESIA shall
identify the type of habitat at the Project site along with the nature of biodiversity available both floral
and fauna species.

Map and screen the area of influence against protected areas, critical habitat, Key Biodiversity Areas
(KBAs), Important Bird and Biodiversity Areas, Ramsar sites, and other sensitive receptors; define
exclusion criteria if needed, aligned with the ESCP. Identify biodiversity-related associated facilities
and cumulative impact pathways across the irrigation command and downstream receiving
environments

ESS7 Indigenous Peoples/Sub-Saharan African Historically Underserved Traditional Local Communities:
Identify whether Indigenous Peoples/Sub-Saharan African Historically Underserved Traditional Local
Communities (IP/SSAHUTLCs) are present in, or have collective attachment to the project area, and assess
differentiated risks, impacts, and opportunities. Assess whether any of the three FPIC circumstances may
arise: (a) adverse impacts on land/natural resources subject to traditional ownership or customary use;
(b) relocation from such lands/resources; or (c) significant impacts on cultural heritage material to HUP
identity. If so, propose an FPIC roadmap with roles, timelines aligned to community decision cycles,
documentation, and verification, proportionate to risk.

ESS8 Cultural Heritage: Define the project’s area of influence and screen for the presence or potential of
cultural heritage (tangible and intangible) using desk review, authority databases, and reconnaissance.
Describe legal protection status, character-defining elements, and significance (local, national,
international). Include sensitivity to vibration, dewatering, changes in flow regime, and access restrictions
that may arise from construction and operational changes. Prepare clear, project- and contract-ready
Chance Finds Procedures (CFP) covering stop-work, securing the find, notification protocol, expert
assessment, authority coordination, documentation, decision-making, and works resumption.

ESS10 Stakeholder Engagement and Information Disclosure: Identify and analyze project-affected parties
and other interested parties, including disadvantaged/vulnerable groups (e.g., women irrigators, landless
households, persons with disabilities, pastoralists/mobile users, linguistic minorities), mapping interests,
influence, information needs, preferred channels, and barriers to participation. Plan and conduct inclusive
consultations at, scoping, baseline/impact assessment, and draft ESIA/ESMP disclosure. Disclose plain-
language project briefs, maps, and ESIA findings in timely and accessible formats and languages; use
multiple channels (kebele/woreda offices, WUA boards, community meetings, local radio, web and social
media where appropriate).Design a GM accessible to all affected parties at no cost, allowing anonymous
submissions and free of retaliation; include procedures for intake, registration, acknowledgment,
assessment, resolution, and feedback within defined timelines. Maintain a documented record of
engagement: stakeholder map, invitations, agendas, minutes, participant demographics (with consent),
photo logs, geo-referenced notes as relevant, issues–response matrices, and GM logs.
ii. Risks related to development and operation of ancillary facilities (if any).
    •   The risks/impacts shall be segregated by Subproject phases, i.e., (i) pre-construction, (ii) construction, (iii)
        operation and maintenance, and (iv) decommissioning.
    •   In addition to the direct and/or indirect impacts, the cumulative impacts (aggregate, incremental, and
        synergistic impacts) of Subproject implementation in the Subproject area of influence shall be
        comprehensively identified. The cumulative Subproject impacts shall be distinguished between their
        national, regional, and local effects.
    •   The environmental and social impacts shall consider identified challenges during Subproject planning,
        design, and implementation.
    •   The identified environmental and social impacts shall be prioritized based on their risk significance,
        magnitude, spatial and temporal extent.

iii. Cumulative Impact assessment
Describe the subproject sites, their siting, construction schedule, operational features and other projects
and activities that may give rise to cumulative effects. The consultant will use all the available information
including consultation with relevant authorities, ground information. The cumulative impact assessment
will need to sequence planned developments of the project, and ancillary and local infrastructure. The
consultants will need to propose geographic and temporal boundaries based on the screening of potential
impacts on key environmental components. The geographic context should include administrative,
watershed and transboundary context or any other suitable context.

The consultant is expected to identify key-related contributions to cumulative effects on selected
resources of concern, such as on biodiversity, habitat loss and fragmentation; changes in hydrology and
water availability due to multiple irrigation schemes; water quality degradation: cumulative discharge of
agricultural runoff, pesticides, and fertilizers from expanded irrigated areas; soil salinization and land
degradation: an incremental loss of access to natural resources, cultural sites, and traditional livelihoods
may disproportionately affect indigenous peoples and historically underserved communities; pressures on
local markets, labor, and social dynamics etc.

Assess the level of cumulative effects; determine the significance of cumulative effects; provide
recommendations: Analyze reasonable, feasible options for mitigating or avoiding contribution to any
significant cumulative effects, at the project level and at the national level beyond the scope of the CRISP
project.

ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL MITIGATION MEASURES
Potential environmental and social risks/impacts that will result from Subproject implementation and
operation shall be provided with recommendations on impacts enhancement or mitigation measures. The
recommended measures shall be based on the risk mitigation hierarchy of avoidance, reduction,
mitigation, and compensation/offsetting. The proposed mitigation measures shall bring the adverse
impacts to the levels required by national standards and guidelines, the World Bank ESF and EHS
guidelines, and good international industrial practice (GIIP). The recommended mitigation measures shall
also be commensurate with the level or significance of the adverse impacts. The mitigation measures
should have operational details to enable their implementation. The mitigation measures shall be
presented as environmental and social requirements or clauses to be included in contractors’ works
contract.

ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL MANAGEMENT AND MONITORING PLANS
Environmental and Social Management Plans (ESMPs) and related site-specific plans are essential tools for
ensuring that irrigation rehabilitation projects are carried out sustainably, safely, and in compliance with
legal and policy frameworks. They provide a structured approach to identifying, mitigating, and monitoring
potential environmental and social (E&S) risks and impacts across all project phases. The main site-specific
plan required under the study are
    •   Occupational health and safety plan,
    •   Community health and safety plan including Traffic Management Plan,
    •   Site Rehabilitation and Restoration Plan
    •   Construction Waste Management Plan.
    •   SEA/SH action plan (consideration of the main Project GBV/SEA/SH Action plan)
    •   Security risk management plan (SMP) (Contextualizing the main Project SMP)
    •   Stakeholder consultation and engagement Plan (contextualizing the main Project SEP)
    •   Water use plan (particularly for groundwater-based projects and where water is critical in the basins)
    •   Integrated pest management plan (IPMP)
    •   Irrigation salinity management plan

A comprehensive ESMP typically includes the following key components:
    • Project Description: Overview of the project, site conditions, and key activities.
    • Summary of Potential Impacts: Identification and evaluation of site-specific environmental and
       social risks and impacts.
    • Mitigation Measures: Specific actions to avoid, minimize, or offset adverse impacts, tailored to
       both environmental and social aspects.
    • Monitoring Program: Framework for tracking implementation and effectiveness of mitigation
       measures, including indicators, frequency, and responsibilities.
    • Institutional Arrangements: Defined roles and responsibilities for project staff, contractors, and
       stakeholders involved in ESMP implementation.
    • Capacity Building and Training: Programs to strengthen the skills and knowledge required for
       effective management of E&S issues.
    • Emergency Preparedness and Response: Procedures to address accidental spills, natural disasters,
       or other emergencies.
    • Budget and Resources: Allocation of financial and human resources necessary for ESMP
       implementation and monitoring.
    • Stakeholder Engagement and Grievance Mechanism: Plans for ongoing stakeholder consultation,
       information disclosure, and mechanisms for addressing complaints and concerns (This may need
       to refer to the main Project Stakeholder engagement Plan).
    • Reporting and Review: Systems for documenting progress, lessons learned, and continuous
       improvement of management practices.
    • Together, the ESMP and its associated technical site-specific plans ensure that irrigation
       rehabilitation projects not only comply with regulatory requirements but also deliver long-term
       benefits for both people and the environment.
    • The monitoring plan for Subproject implementation and operation shall be recommended as part
       of the ESIA. Main components of the monitoring plan are:
    • Potential environmental and social benefits and impacts resulting from Subproject
       implementation (identified in the earlier exercise)
    • Recommended enhancement and mitigation measures
    • Key Subproject parameters or aspects to monitor
    • Specific and measurable indicators
    • Monitoring location, if applicable
    • Measurement methods and/or equipment
    •   Frequency of monitoring
    •   Responsible institutions/parties for monitoring
    •   Monitoring budget.

CAPACITY DEVELOPMENT AND TRAINING
For effective implementation of environmental and social risk management, the existing institutional
capacity of the concerned parties shall be assessed and measures to strengthen their capacities (such as
training) shall be recommended as well as a capacity development and training budget.

ANALYSIS OF ALTERNATIVES
Systematically compare feasible alternatives to the proposed project site, technology, design, and
operation—including the “without project” situation—in terms of their potential environmental and social
impacts.

Assesses the alternatives’ feasibility of mitigating the environmental and social impacts; the capital and
recurrent costs of alternative mitigation measures, and their suitability under local conditions; and the
institutional, training, and monitoring requirements for the alternative mitigation measures.

For each of the alternatives, quantifies the environmental and social impacts to the extent possible, and
attaches economic values where feasible.

The ESIA shall identify (along with the Subproject design team) various Subproject implementation
alternatives including:
    •   The ‘no-action’ option which considers maintaining the current status quo in the Subproject area of
        influence
    •   The ‘proposed Subproject’ option including all of its technologies, facilities, and activities
    •   ‘Other options’ to be identified as part of the ESIA which are deemed to achieve the overall objectives of
        the Subproject while using different technology, methodology, and facilities.
    •   A multi-criteria approach shall be used for analysis of the identified Subproject alternatives.

STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION, CONSULTATIONS, INFORMATION DISCLOSURE AND GRIEVANCE REDRESS MECHANISM
Stakeholder identification and analysis shall be done as part of the ESIAs with the objective of involving
the stakeholders in the decision-making process. The main tasks include:
    •   Identify Subproject stakeholders including government bodies/authorities, beneficiary communities,
        Indigenous People, community leaders, civil society organizations (CSOs), non-governmental organizations
        (NGOs), women groups, youth groups, academia, vulnerable groups, etc.
    •   Disclose Subproject information to the stakeholders
    •   Consult on stakeholders’ understanding, views, aspirations, and recommendations on the Subproject and
        on E&S risks and impacts and potential mitigation measures.
    •   Recommend ways to integrate the findings of the stakeholder engagement into the Subproject plan, design,
        and implementation.
    •   Subproject stakeholders may have grievances during implementation and operation stages and thus
        stakeholders should be given a means to voice their grievances and obtain redress. The ESIA process will
        create awareness of the Project Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) that has been adopted as part of the
        overall Project. During consultations stakeholders can be consulted on the available GRM channels and
        potential improvements.

RECOMMENDATIONS
The ESIAs shall provide recommendations based on the findings of the assessment. The recommendations
shall, among other points, include:
    •   Promoting integration and collaboration on environmental and social management by the various
        stakeholders participating during the Subproject implementation and operation;
    •   Strengthening institutions responsible for implementation and management of environmental and social
        issues;
    •   Capacity building needs for institutions responsible for environmental and social management;
    •   Dissemination and disclosure of environmental and social information;
    •   Environmental and social requirements and management measures to be included in the works
        procurement and contract documents;
    •   Recommendations on other key environmental and social management issues.

APPROVAL
The Consultant must submit the final ESIA report for each Subproject, independently to be disclosed
separately. Before the ESIA documents are approved by EPA and the Bank, the consultant is accountable
for carrying out any changes that the client, authorities, may request, and they must keep working until
there are no World Bank, PIU and EPA objections.

APPROACHES AND METHODOLOGIES
The approach and methodology for the ESIAs follows the established pattern for infrastructure projects.
The following section provides the highlight of the approach and methodology that should be adopted for
the ESIA.

DESK REVIEW:
The consultant must hold a briefing meeting with client in the first days after signing the contract.
Subsequently, the consultant shall prepare assessment tools, formats, checklists for primary data
collections instruments at all levels (district, regional and federal level). Besides, the consultant will also
systematically review all relevant secondary data (e.g., The GoE financing agreement provisions, pertinent
documents related to Environmental and social issues, Legal frameworks, project Appraisal Reports,
guidelines, project E&S instruments (Stakeholder Engagement Plan, Environmental and Social
Commitment Plan).

SITE VISIT:
In consideration of the nature and the location of the potential future conditions and consequences, the
Study area will be identified. This will be the Project area in which implementation will definitely bring
about measurable and sometimes significant direct changes to the physical environment and ecology, as
well as the social and economic conditions. This will include downstream considerations.

Collection of Available Information: to identify potential sources of the impact of the project on the
environment and the consultants collected publications, laws and regulations, and census reports.
Information on existing environmental conditions, necessary to provide the background for impact
identification and assessment will obtain from the published sources and field visits.

Maps and Images: the consultant should use the topographic maps, Satellite images and area map used
to identify and delineate villages, resources and facilities that could potentially be affected in the proposed
scheme and to assess the land use, vegetation cover, infrastructure, settlements, and other socio-
economic activities within the area of influence.
Field Investigation: the field investigation on physical resources, ecological resources, socio-economic
aspects, health, cultural and other values in the project area will be collect.

Public Consultations: The consultant should conduct the consultations with various community members
in the project-affected area.

Description of the Environment and Social Baseline: Information on the existing natural and socio-
economic resources is of fundamental importance for evaluation of environmental impacts. So the
consultant should collect the baseline data on the physical, biological and social, cultural and socio-
economic setting of the project will be assemble, evaluate and present.

Identification of Environmental and Social Risks and Impacts: Key beneficial as well as adverse impacts
on the physical, biological and socio-economic environment associated with the
construction/rehabilitation and operation phases of the project will be identify and quantify where
possible.

Environmental and Social Mitigation and Benefit Enhancement Measures: Feasible and cost-effective
mitigation and benefit enhancement measures that may avoid or reduce potentially significant adverse
environmental impacts to acceptable levels shall be identified and recommended.

IMPLEMENTATION ARRANGEMENT
The Consultant will work closely with the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) in MILLS and the
Environmental, Health and Safety Department. The Consultant’s team leader will be the principal contact.
The Consultant will be responsible for all aspects of performance of services set forth in the TOR.

The PIU will be responsible for providing the information and supporting arrangement for the necessary
field investigations and consultations. The PIU will provide the following facilities and documents:
    •   All the relevant documents, including the Draft ESIA and feasibility studies;
    •   The Subproject information and design prepared to date;
    •   Supporting letter for field work, and
    •   Access to relevant information to the extent of its availability.

The Consultant shall hold periodic progress review meetings with the Client at least once in a month.
Additional meetings shall be scheduled if necessary. The intent of these meetings will be for the Client to
provide input and to discuss options for addressing the Client's comments. The Consultant shall fully
cooperate with the Client in scheduling and attending such meetings as requested by the Client. The Client
will be responsible to prepare meeting minutes during each of these meetings. Minutes will be distributed
to participants for review and comment. The consultant is also required to make presentation to higher
management of the Project implementing agencies and the World Bank as and when necessary.

DELIVERABLES

Reports

The reports are to be written in English language and should explain the activities performed in detail up
to the satisfaction of the client. . The consultant must provide the client with the following reports:
    •     Inception Report: Inception Report shall be submitted, reflecting the agreed methodology, techniques,
          schedule, deliverables and an outline of the proposed contents of the assignment.
    •     Draft Report: In a stakeholder workshop, the consultant must present the ESIAs and take feedback into
          account. Deliveries must be based on the client's priorities.
    •     Final Report: All issues brought up during stakeholder engagements, as well as the comments of the client
          and bank shall be covered in the final report.
    •     Progress Report: The Consultant shall furnish the PIU with a written Weekly Progress Report that
          summarizes all aspects of the completed monthly and cumulative work progress. The objectives of the
          Progress Report are to:
    •     Provide a reliable and readily accessible summary record of the project activities with daily activities
          performed by each individual at the site and progress of work during last week with verification of daily task
          in the site signed by the project's representative.
    •     Provide a detailed description of all work actually completed up to the date and revision to the project
          schedule required, which shall reflect changes in the critical path since the date of the last revision.
    •   Identify issues and problems requiring action by the PIU or the Consultant, including issues of conflicting
        priorities.
    •   Provide a forecast of the work to be accomplished in the next month and information to help substantiate
        the Consultant’s pay request.

REPORTS

Submittal Protocol

No later than seven (07) days after the Commencement Date, the Consultant shall submit a submittal
protocol for the Consulting Services. The submittal protocol shall identify the submittal documents/reports
to be prepared by the Consultant including but not limited to detailed listing of the content, the expected
dates of the submittals, number of copies, and distribution of the submittals by the Consultant based on
distribution information provided by the Client.

The Consultant has obligation to provide submittals for review consistent with the submittal dates. The
Consultant acknowledges that the Client’s review will often involve input from, or consultation with, a
number of individuals. Therefore, should submittal dates to the Client be delayed, the Consultant shall
provide prompt notice to the Client of the delay. In no case shall this notice be given less than five (05)
calendar days prior to the scheduled submittal date for that submittal. The submittals shall identify any
proposed change to the requirements, or the design concept, project delivery approach, or the project
schedule provided in the Consultant’s proposal, accompanied by the rationale behind the proposed
change. No changes shall be implemented without the Client’s acceptance. Such acceptance shall not,
however, relieve the Consultant of any of its obligations under the contract.

Report Outline

The reports outline should provide information that will enable the Bank and clients to identify, evaluate
and manage environmental and social, health and safety risks relating to the projects. The reports should
be with the outline acceptable to local competent authorities (EPA), and World Bank ESS.
Executive summaries, a list of the projects affected and their socioeconomic profiles, maps, meeting
minutes, and some relevant photos must be included in all reports (aside from the progress report).
Reporting Format

Reports for each project must be submitted in one main volume (A4 format) and with the appropriate
number of appendices, drawings, photographs, and other exhibits in additional volumes, in either A4 or
A3 format as needed.

The Submittal form for each document is electronical (both PDF as well as editable version) and in hard
copies as may be required by the approving Authorities in Ethiopia (EPA), with a cover letter to the PIU.
The PIU is responsible for distribution of submittals to reviewers. Electronic submittals shall be in the
original file format. The Consultant is responsible for the accuracy and completeness of the information
submitted.

Client Review of Reports

The PIU will review submittals for consistency with the methodology concept presented in the Consultant’s
proposal. The primary purpose of the Client’s review is to satisfy itself that the submittals generally
conform to the intent of the contract. The Client’s review shall not relieve the Consultant of the sole risk
and responsibility for all defects, errors or omissions, or of sole responsibility for meeting all requirements
of the contract.

The Consultant shall make submittals far enough in advance of subsequent activities to allow time for
reviews, consultations with other entities, for securing necessary acceptance, for possible revisions and
re-submittals. The Client intends to process the submittals as quickly as practical.


Finalization of the Reports

Following approval of the final report by the PIU, the World Bank and EPA, the Consultant shall make
necessary revisions and finalize the report for public disclosure. The approved report prepared for
disclosure shall be submitted in electronic copy to be distributed to the project stakeholders.

QUALIFICATION OF CONSULTANT
Consultant’s Organization and Experience

Submit a brief description of the Consultant’s organization and an outline of the recent experience of the
Consultant that is most relevant to the assignment. In the case of a joint venture, information on similar
assignments shall be provided for each partner. For each assignment, the outline should indicate the
names of the Consultant’s Key Experts and Sub-consultants who participated, the duration of the
assignment, the contract amount (total and, if it was done in a form of a joint venture or a sub-consultancy,
the amount paid to the Consultant), and the Consultant’s role/involvement.
Qualification of Key-Staff

The Consultant shall assemble a team of both key and non-key experts as per the Table-2 below. List of
Experts (LOE) and estimates are indicative, and Consultants are free to propose their own LOE to match
their methodology and staffing plans, noting any deviations from the notional LOE as well as the reasons
behind them. Additional staff and staffing requirements may be proposed by the Consultant as necessary
and based on the Consultant’s proposed methodology and approach that will achieve the objectives of
the assignment. Consultant shall provide and maintain all Key Personnel. Once the LOE is approved by the
PIU, any changes are subject to prior approvals by the Project implementing agencies in accordance with
the terms of the contract. International staff with the required expertise and based internationally can
also be proposed if they are able to demonstrate required country expertise.

The consultant will be expected to work closely with the PIU staff, in particular the Environmental and
Social Specialists and other third parties relevant to the required services, particularly relevant
representatives from the Regional and local Governments where those projects are going to be built.
Therefore, the consultant will also be expected to have good communication, writing, and analytical skills
as well as have a good command of languages, namely English.

Backstopping Staff: The Consultant shall ensure and demonstrate in the proposal that the firm has the
expertise not explicitly covered in the above list but needed for the assignment. In addition to the key staff
positions above, the consultant shall include in its proposal the CVs of a pool of experts that would be
assigned to the study as and when required, including a contract management staff member (the pool of
experts will not be included in the evaluation of key staff positions but are required to demonstrate the
firms access to a pool of experts).
